Refuting Might as Truth: Critique of Marx's Criterion
Author refutes Marx's victory criterion using colonialism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that victory is a reliable sign of truth by providing a historical counterexample from Islamic history.

Core Idea
Might does not make right; truth is independent of worldly victory.

Karl Marx proposed that victory is the sign of truth and defeat the sign of falsehood. The author rejects this by pointing to colonial domination: the fact that a colonial power is victorious does not make its cause just. In this lesson, we will analyse this refutation and apply it to Islamic history to see how truth often stands with the oppressed, not the powerful.

Guided Path
1
Identify Marx's claim
State clearly: Marx says victory (al-ghalaba) is the sign of truth, defeat is the sign of falsehood.
2
Locate the author's counterexample
The author mentions colonial states dominating poor countries: does the mere fact of control make the oppressor right and the oppressed wrong? Obviously not.
This exposes the flaw in equating success with truth.
3
Find an Islamic historical parallel
Consider the Battle of Uhud: the polytheists defeated the Muslims, but that did not make their falsehood true. Similarly, the early Muslims were often outnumbered but their cause remained true.
Victory in battle is not a criterion of truth in Islam.
4
Formulate the general principle
Truth is determined by evidence and revelation, not by power or outcome. The author's refutation shows that empirical reality contradicts Marx's criterion.
This is a fundamental epistemological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه ) أجابة الفيلسوف الالماني كارل ماركس { ۱۸۱۸م-۱۸۸۳م علامة الحق وآيته والهزيمة هي ملاك الباطل وعلمه الغلبة هي تعليق ان وقائع التاريخ مثلا ترفض هذا اللون من التفكير فلو نظرنا الى سيطرة الدول الاستعمارية على الدول الفقيرة فهل مجرد السيطرة تعطي الحق للظالم المحتل، أو ان المظلوم الذي سلبت ارضه ووطنه اصبح باطلا .
5) The answer of the German philosopher Karl Marx (1818-1883): The sign and token of truth is victory, and defeat is the standard of falsehood and its sign. Comment: Historical facts, for example, reject this way of thinking. If we look at the control of colonial states over poor countries, does mere control give right to the oppressor occupier, or does the oppressed who had his land and homeland taken become false?
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
Marx's criterion of victory is refuted by the historical example of colonialism: the fact that a colonial power is victorious does not make its cause true. Might does not make right. The author exposes the flaw in equating success with truth.

Key Terms
الغلبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is victory not a reliable sign of truth?
A) Because victory is often due to chance
B) Because the polytheists' victory over Muslims at Uhud did not make their falsehood true
C) Because defeat can also be a sign of truth
D) Because no historical example supports it

Key Takeaway
Victory or defeat is not a criterion for truth; truth is determined by evidence and revelation, not by power or outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another historical example from Islamic history where the side that was victorious was false, and explain why this contradicts Marx's criterion. (e.g., the victory of the Umayyads over Imam Hussein at Karbala did not make their cause true.)
?
Refute Marx's criterion using the example of the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) in Makkah: he and his followers were persecuted and defeated for years, yet their truth was never in doubt.

The Criterion of Truth: Self-Evidence or Something More?
Author critiques the Aristotelian-Islamic view of self-evident knowledge as criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the critique that self-evident knowledge (badihiyya) is not the ultimate criterion of truth and identify the unanswered question that the Aristotelian-Islamic view leaves unresolved.

Core Idea
Self-evidence is not self-certifying; who certifies the self-evident?

In Islamic logic and philosophy, self-evident knowledge (badihiyya) is often presented as the foundation of certainty. Aristotle and the Islamic philosophers argued that intuitive ideas are the criterion for distinguishing truth from falsehood. However, a deeper question emerges: what guarantees that self-evident ideas themselves are true? This lesson examines the critique that the Aristotelian criterion simply postpones the question without providing a meta-criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Aristotelian-Islamic claim
Find the passage where the author states that self-evident knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-badihiyya) is the criterion for distinguishing truth from falsehood. Note that they define truth as correspondence to reality.
The phrase 'تجليات البصائر' refers to the manifestations of insight.
2
Extract the definition of truth
Locate the definition: a true proposition is one that corresponds to reality (al-muttabaqa li-l-waqi'), and a false one contradicts it.
This is the correspondence theory of truth.
3
Locate the author's objection
Identify the 'essential question' (السؤال الجوهري) that the author says has not been raised: 'What is the standard by which a thing is true or illusory?' and 'Who decides that a proposition corresponds to reality?'
The author claims the answer (self-evidence) is an evasion, not a solution.
4
Analyze the critique's structure
The author points out that appealing to self-evidence leads to a regress: if self-evident ideas are the criterion, what certifies that they are true? This is a meta-criterion problem.
This critique is epistemological: it questions foundationalism.
5
Apply to Islamic epistemology
Consider how this critique applies to foundational claims in Kalam, such as 'The world is temporally originated' or 'Every event has a cause'. Are these self-evident? If they are challenged, what is the ultimate ground?
This shows the relevance of the critique to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ ) أجابة ارسطو ومعه الفلاسفة الاسلاميون : ان المعرفة البدهية (تجليات البصائر) هي المعيار فهي الحجر الاساس لتشخيص الحق عن الباطل والصواب عن الخطأ . ان الافكار البدهية في باطن العقل التي يذعن لها الانسان من دون الحاجة الى برهان هي معيار الحقيقة والوهم . فالقضية الصادقة المخالفة له . أن المطابقة للواقع والكاذبة هي السؤال الجوهري لم يطرح بعد وهو : ما الملاك الذي يكون به الشيء حقا او وهما ؟ من الذي يقرر ان القضية صادقة مطابقة للواقع ومعبرة عنه أم لا ؟ من الذي يميز المفيد والناجح عن الضار والفاشل ان المحاولات آنفة الذكر هي هروب من الجواب وليس جوابا .
6) The answer of Aristotle and with him the Islamic philosophers: Intuitive knowledge (manifestations of insights) is the criterion; it is the foundation for distinguishing truth from falsehood and right from wrong. Intuitive ideas in the depth of the mind, which a person accepts without need for proof, are the criterion of truth and illusion. So the true proposition is the one that corresponds to reality and the false is the one that contradicts it. The essential question has not yet been raised: What is the standard by which a thing is true or illusory? Who decides that a proposition is true, corresponding to reality and expressing it or not? Who distinguishes the useful and successful from the harmful and failing? These aforementioned attempts are an escape from the answer, not an answer.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the Aristotelian and Islamic philosopher's view that self-evident knowledge (badihi) is the criterion. They define truth as correspondence to reality. However, the author objects that this answer is insufficient because it does not specify the ultimate standard that guarantees that self-evident ideas are true. The question remains: what makes something true? The author dismisses all earlier answers as evasions.

Key Terms
المعرفة البدهية
الباطل
الصدق
المطابقة للواقع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the problem with the Aristotelian criterion of self-evident knowledge?
A) Self-evident knowledge cannot be distinguished from falsehood.
B) The criterion fails because it does not provide a standard for judging which self-evident ideas are true.
C) Correspondence to reality is an invalid definition of truth.
D) Islamic philosophers reject self-evident knowledge altogether.

Key Takeaway
The appeal to self-evidence as the criterion of truth leaves unanswered the fundamental question of what certifies self-evidence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a standard Kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' The first premise is often argued from empirical observation, but is it self-evident? Apply the author's critique: what would you say to someone who questions whether temporal origination itself is a valid concept? Where would you ground your response?
?
In Islamic epistemology, the first principles of logic (e.g., the law of non-contradiction) are considered badihiyyat. Using the author's critique, construct a response to someone who says these principles are merely cultural conventions. How can a Muslim scholar defend the absolute truth of these principles without appealing to self-evidence alone?

The Quranic Balance (Mizan) as the Criterion for Truth
Author introduces the Quranic concept of mizan as the internal criterion for truth, then raises the problem of error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of the Quranic mizan as an internal faculty for distinguishing truth from falsehood and identify the question of error that arises.

Core Idea
The Quranic mizan is the soul's innate balance that separates truth from falsehood.

This lesson introduces the author's unique epistemological proposal: the Quranic concept of mizan (balance) as an innate faculty of the soul that receives knowledge and evaluates it. The passage then raises a classic question about the origin of error, which has occupied philosophers and logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary claim about mizan
The author states that the question of the criterion for truth has no answer except in the Quranic mizan. Recognize that this is a strong claim: the mizan is the ultimate internal standard.
2
Extract the functions of mizan
The passage describes three functions: receives all elements of knowledge, compares between right and wrong, and presents something like a report. List these functions.
3
Locate the transition to the problem of error
After describing mizan, the author shifts to ask: how does error arise in human thought? This is a separate but related question that philosophers and logicians have debated.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the mizan being innate (in the soul) matters. It implies that humans are equipped with a natural capacity for truth, but error still needs explanation. This sets the stage for the author's later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان هذا السؤال لاجواب له الا في مفهوم الميزان الذي طرحه القرآن الكريم : فالميزان عنصر جوهري من مكونات النفس وله اهمية بالغة فهو يستلم جميع عناصر المعرفة ويقارن بين الصواب والخطأ ويقدم ما يشبه تقريرا ومن الأمور المهمة التي يجب الإشارة إليها هو في كيفية نشأة الخطأ في الفكر البشري وقد اثار هذا التساؤل كثير من الفلاسفة والمناطقة واجابوا باجابات متعددة.
Indeed, this question has no answer except in the concept of the balance (mizan) that the Holy Quran introduced. For the balance is an essential element of the components of the soul, and it has extreme importance. It receives all elements of knowledge, compares between right and wrong, and presents something like a report. Among the important matters that must be pointed out is how error originates in human thought. Many philosophers and logicians have raised this question and answered with multiple answers.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the author's own solution: the Quranic mizan, an innate faculty in the human soul, is the criterion that processes knowledge and distinguishes truth from falsehood. Then the author transitions to a new topic: the origin of error, which has been debated by philosophers and logicians. This sets up the next section of the work.

D) It stores knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic mizan is the soul's innate balance for evaluating knowledge, and the origin of error remains an open question.

Divine Revelation as a Source of Knowledge
Author asserts that divine scriptures and prophets are fundamental sources of knowledge, citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic verses cited to support revelation as a source of knowledge and explain how prophets serve as conveyors of that knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine revelation is a primary source of knowledge, transmitting facts about all domains.

This passage establishes that divine scriptures (Torah, Gospel, Quran) and prophets are fundamental sources of knowledge. The author supports this claim by citing several Quranic verses that describe the scriptures as guidance and light, the Quran as a clarification of all things, and prophets as teachers of the Book and wisdom. Understanding this epistemologically is crucial for recognizing the role of revelation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
The author states that divine scriptures and prophets are a basic source (masdar asasi) of knowledge. This is the thesis of the passage.
The claim is not argued logically but supported by scriptural evidence.
2
Locate the Quranic verses cited
Find the Quranic references: Torah and Gospel contain guidance and light; Quran is a clarification of everything; prophets are sent with their people's language; the Prophet Muhammad teaches the Book and wisdom.
These verses are quoted as evidence for the claim.
3
Analyze how each verse supports the claim
Each verse emphasizes that revelation provides knowledge: Torah and Gospel give guidance and light; Quran explains all things; messengers clarify in the language of their people; the Prophet recites, purifies, and teaches.
The variety of verses shows the comprehensiveness of revealed knowledge.
4
Understand the role of prophets
Prophets are not only transmitters of scriptures but also teachers who explain and purify. They are a source of knowledge through their instruction.
Prophets are an intermediary source, but the ultimate source is divine revelation.
5
Consider the broader epistemological implication
The passage implies that human knowledge can be derived from revelation, which supplements reason and empirical observation. This is a key tenet of Islamic epistemology.
This contrasts with purely rationalist or empiricist epistemologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكتب السماوية والانبياء هي مصدر أساس من مصادر المعرفة كما تقول آيات القرآن الكريم. لقد زود الانسان بعدد هائل من الحقائق التاريخية والاجتماعية والعلمية والثقافية والاقتصادية من خلال الكتب السماوية مما ساعده في رسم مفاهيم أصيلة للكون والخلق والحياة والتطور حيث الفت كتب غزيرة واكتشفت علوما جمة. وعن هذا المصدر المعرفي، قال القرآن الكريم (إنا أنزلنا التوراة فيها هدى ونور) (وآتيناه الإنجيل فيه هدى ونور) وفي الخطاب الموجه الى النبي محمد (ونزلنا عليك الكتاب تبيانا لكل شيء) (وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم) (هو الذي بعث في الأميين رسولا منهم يتلو عليهم آياته ويزكيهم ويعلمهم الكتاب والحكمة وإن كانوا من قبل لفي ضلال مبين). وسنذكر بعض الحقائق العلمية التي تزود بها البشر من خلال القرآن الكريم لكي تكتمل ملامح الصورة.
Divine scriptures and the prophets are a basic source of knowledge, as the verses of the Noble Quran say. Man has been endowed with an enormous number of historical, social, scientific, cultural, and economic facts through the divine scriptures, which helped him formulate original concepts of the universe, creation, life, and development, leading to the writing of abundant books and the discovery of many sciences. And regarding this source of knowledge, the Noble Quran said: 'Indeed, We sent down the Torah, in which was guidance and light.' And 'We gave him the Gospel, in which was guidance and light.' And in the address to Prophet Muhammad: 'And We have sent down to you the Book as a clarification of all things.' 'And We have not sent any messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them.' 'It is He who has sent among the unlettered a messenger from themselves, reciting to them His verses and purifying them and teaching them the Book and wisdom, although they were before in manifest error.' And we will mention some scientific facts that mankind has been equipped with through the Noble Quran so that the features of the picture are complete.

Commentary
This passage classifies divine scriptures (kutub samawiyya) and prophets as fundamental sources of knowledge (masadir al-ma'rifa). The author supports this with Quranic verses: Torah and Gospel contain guidance and light; the Quran is a clarification (tibyan) of everything; prophets were sent with their people's language for clear communication; and the Prophet Muhammad teaches the Book and wisdom. The passage then transitions to listing scientific facts from the Quran as further evidence. The author emphasizes the comprehensiveness of scriptural knowledge in various fields.

Divine scriptures, especially the Quran, are presented as a comprehensive source of knowledge encompassing all domains, supported by Quranic verses that highlight their guidance and clarification.

Scientific Facts in the Quran: Epistemological Evaluation
Author lists three scientific facts from Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the epistemological strength of using Quranic verses as evidence for divine knowledge, by analyzing the three examples in this passage.

Core Idea
The Quran's alignment with modern science is used to argue for its divine origin.

This passage presents three Quranic verses that describe phenomena later confirmed by modern science: atmospheric pressure, wind pollination, and honey's healing properties. The author uses these as examples of scientific knowledge in the Quran. In this lesson, we will learn how to identify such claims and critically assess their role in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three claims
Read the Arabic and translation to identify the three scientific facts: decreased atmospheric pressure at altitude, wind pollination of plants, and honey as a cure for diseases.
Each fact is introduced by 'حقيقة' in Arabic.
2
Link each fact to its Quranic verse
For each fact, note the corresponding verse: (1) atmospheric pressure in Surah al-An'am 6:125, (2) wind pollination in Surah al-Hijr 15:22, (3) honey healing in Surah al-Nahl 16:68-69.
The verses are quoted in the passage.
3
Assess the relationship between verse and scientific fact
Determine whether the verse explicitly states the scientific mechanism or only allows an interpretation. For example, the chest tightness at altitude is a metaphor for disbelief, but it can be read as physical description. Wind pollination is explicitly mentioned. Honey's healing is stated directly.
Some verses may have multiple interpretations; evaluate the strength of the link.
4
Reconstruct the implicit epistemological argument
The author implies: Since the Quran mentions facts unknown at the time, and these are later confirmed, the Quran must be from a divine source. Identify the premises and conclusion of this argument.
This is an inductive argument from specific cases to a general conclusion.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
Consider possible objections: Are the interpretations anachronistic? Could the knowledge have been acquired naturally? Does a few examples prove divine origin? Reflect on the logical validity and epistemological weight.
This step develops critical thinking about such claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقيقة : انخفاض الضغط الجوي عند ارتفاع الانسان الى السماء. قال تعالى (فمن يرد الله أن يهديه يشرح صدره للإسلام ومن يرد أن يضله يجعل صدره ضيقا حرجا كأنما يصعد في السماء). حقيقة : ان بعض النباتات تلقح بوساطة الرياح (وأرسلنا الرياح لواقح). حقيقة : ان عسل النحل علاج شاف لبعض الامراض (وأوحى ربك إلى النحل أن اتخذي من الجبال بيوتا ومن الشجر ومما يعرشون. ثم كلي من كل الثمرات فاسلكي سبل ربك ذللا يخرج من بطونها شراب مختلف ألوانه فيه شفاء للناس إن في ذلك لآية لقوم يتفكرون).
Fact: The decrease in atmospheric pressure when a person ascends to the sky. He said, exalted: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his breast to Islam; and whoever He wants to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted, as if he were climbing up into the sky.' Fact: Some plants are pollinated by means of winds. 'And We sent the winds as pollinating.' Fact: Honeybee honey is a healing cure for some diseases. 'And your Lord inspired the bee, saying: Take for yourselves houses in the mountains and in the trees and in what they erect. Then eat from all the fruits and follow the ways of your Lord laid down easy. There comes forth from their bellies a drink of varying colors, in which there is healing for people. Indeed, in that is a sign for a people who give thought.'

This passage presents three scientific facts explicitly mentioned in the Quran as evidence of its knowledge content. First: the phenomenon of chest tightness at high altitude corresponds to low atmospheric pressure, referenced in Surah al-An'am (6:125). Second: wind pollination of plants, from Surah al-Hijr (15:22). Third: the healing properties of honey, from Surah al-Nahl (16:68-69). The author treats these as examples of scientific knowledge provided by revelation, supporting the claim that scriptures are a source of knowledge.

Evaluating Scientific Claims from the Quran
Author lists six scientific truths from Quranic verses as epistemic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the epistemic structure of a Quranic scientific fact citation and determine whether it constitutes a direct proof or an interpreted sign.

Core Idea
The Quran contains signs of creation, not a science textbook.

This passage presents six scientific facts that the author claims are provided by the Quran. As an epistemologist, you need to assess how these facts are derived: are they directly stated, or are they interpretations? This lesson will equip you with the tools to analyze such citations critically, distinguishing between explicit revelation and human inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the verse
For each fact, locate the specific Quranic verse cited. Notice the exact wording of the claim (e.g., 'Fact: decrease in atmospheric pressure').
2
Determine the type of reference
Is the scientific phenomenon explicitly described in the verse, or is it alluded to through metaphor or analogy? For example, the verse about tight chest (6:125) does not mention atmospheric pressure; it uses a simile.
3
Assess the author's inference
Examine how the author connects the verse to the scientific fact. Does the verse require external knowledge to be understood as a scientific claim? If so, the epistemic weight is different from a clear proposition.
4
Evaluate epistemic strength
Decide whether the verse alone suffices as evidence for the fact, or whether it serves as a sign (aya) pointing to a deeper truth that must be verified by empirical science.
5
Consider alternative interpretations
Reflect on whether the verse could be interpreted differently. This step highlights the need for tafsir principles when extracting scientific knowledge from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسنذكر بعض الحقائق العلمية التي تزود بها البشر من خلال القرآن الكريم لكي تكتمل ملامح الصورة .
حقيقة : انخفاض الضغط الجوي عند ارتفاع الانسان الى السماء. قال تعالى (فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشرح صدرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ وَمَنْ يُرِدْ أَنْ يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا كَأَنَّمَا يَصْعَدُ في السَّمَاء)
٦ سورة الانعام الآية ١٢٥
حقيقة : ان بعض النباتات تلقح بوساطة الرياح (وَأَرْسَلْنَا الرِّيَاحَ لَواقِعَ)
۷ سورة الحجر الآية ٢٢
حقيقة : ان عسل النحل علاج شاف لبعض الامراض (وَأَوْحَى رَبُّكَ إِلَى النَّحْلِ أَنِ اتَّخِذِي مِنَ الْجِبَالِ بُيُوتًا وَمِنَ الشَّجَرِ وَمِمَّا يَعرِشُونَ . ثُمّ كُلِي مِنْ كُلِّ الثَّمَرَاتِ فَاسْلُكِي سُبُلَ رَبِّكِ ذللا يَخْرُجُ مِن بُطُونِهَا شَرَابٌ مُختلِف أَلْوَانُهُ فيه شفاء للناس إنّ ذلك لآيَةً لقوم يتفكرون)
۸ سورة النحل الآيتان ٦٨ - ٦٩
حقيقة : ان الجبال تحفظ للارض توازنها في مواجهة الماء (وَأَلْقَى فِي الْأَرْضِ رَوَاسِيَ أَنْ تَمِيدَ بِكُمْ)
۱ سورة النحل الآية ١٥
حقيقة : إن المياه العذبة والمياه المالحة لا تمتزجان عند التلاقي (وَهُوَ الَّذِي مَرَجَ الْبَحرينِ هَذَا عَذَبٌ فُرَات وَهَذَا مِلْحٌ أَجَاج وجعل بينهما برزَحًا وحجرًا محجوراً)
۲ سورة الفرقان الآية ٥٣
حقيقة : أن الارض متحركة وترى الجبال تحسبها جامدة وهي تمر مر السحاب) والجبال ملتصقة بالارض.
۳ سورة النمل الآية ٨٨
And we will mention some scientific truths that humanity has been provided with through the Noble Quran, so that the features of the picture are complete. Fact: The decrease in atmospheric pressure when a human ascends to the sky. He said, the Most High: (So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his breast to Islam; and whoever He wants to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted as if he were ascending into the sky). 6 Surah al-An'am, verse 125. Fact: That some plants are pollinated by wind. (And We sent the winds as pollinating). 7 Surah al-Hijr, verse 22. Fact: That honey is a healing remedy for some diseases. (And your Lord inspired to the bee, 'Take for yourself among the mountains houses and among the trees and of that which they construct. Then eat from all the fruits and follow the ways of your Lord laid down.' There emerges from their bellies a drink of varying colors in which there is healing for people. Indeed, in that is a sign for a people who give thought). 8 Surah an-Nahl, verses 68-69. Fact: That the mountains preserve the earth's balance against water. (And He has cast into the earth firmly rooted mountains so it would not sway with you). 1 Surah an-Nahl, verse 15. Fact: That fresh water and salty water do not mix upon meeting. (And it is He who has released the two seas, one fresh and sweet and one salty and bitter, and made between them a barrier and a forbidding partition). 2 Surah al-Furqan, verse 53. Fact: That the earth moves, and you see the mountains thinking they are stationary while they pass like the passing of clouds. And the mountains are attached to the earth. 3 Surah an-Naml, verse 88.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of six scientific facts derived from the Quran, used by the author as evidence of the Quran's role as a source of knowledge. The author begins by stating the intent to mention these facts. Each fact is presented with a Quranic verse that alludes to the phenomenon. The first fact relates to atmospheric pressure and the difficulty of breathing at high altitudes (Quran 6:125). The second mentions wind pollination (15:22). The third highlights the medicinal properties of honey (16:68-69). The fourth refers to mountains as stabilizers of the earth (16:15). The fifth notes the separation of fresh and salt water (25:53). The sixth mentions the movement of mountains as a sign of the earth's rotation (27:88). The author does not argue these points but presents them as given truths from revelation, implying that the Quran contains scientific knowledge that humanity discovers later.

External and Internal Sources of Knowledge in Quranic Epistemology
Author explains that prophets and scriptures are two springs of knowledge in Quranic epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external and internal sources of knowledge as presented in the Quranic epistemological framework.

Core Idea
The Quran presents two springs of knowledge: external (prophets and scriptures) and internal (innate reason and self-evident truths).

This passage introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic epistemology: knowledge comes from both external revelation and internal rational intuition. The author explains that scriptures require interpretation by prophets, who serve as external sources, while the soul's insights and axiomatic principles function as internal sources. Understanding this dual-source framework is essential for grasping how Islamic scholars integrate revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources of knowledge
In the passage, the author states that prophets and heavenly scriptures are one spring, and the insights of the soul and axiomatic constants are another spring. Note that the external source depends on prophets to explain scripture, while the internal source is inherent.
2
Understand the metaphor of external and internal prophet
The jurists and exegetes compare scriptures and messengers to an 'external prophet' (nabiyy khārijī), and the soul's insights and self-evident truths to an 'internal prophet' (nabiyy dākhilī). This metaphor emphasizes that both are authoritative sources, but one comes from outside and one from within.
This metaphor is not standard in classical mantiq but appears in later Islamic epistemology.
3
Analyze the relationship between the two sources
The passage implies that external sources (scripture) need explanation, which prophets provide. Internal sources (innate reason) operate independently. Together, they form a complete epistemology: revelation guides, and reason understands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الكتب السماوية في حاجة الى بيان معانيها وتوضيحها وكشف المراد منها وكان الانبياء يقومون بهذا الدور ويفيضون على الناس بمزيد من المعلومات في مختلف نواحي الحياة فشكلوا مصدرا للمعرفة كما تقول نظرية القرآن . أن الفقهاء والمفسرين يقولون ان الكتب السماوية والرسل هم بمثابة ) نبي خارجي وان بصائر النفس وثوابتها البديهية هما بمثابة نبي (داخلي) والخلاصة : ان الانبياء والكتب السماوية ينبوعان من ينابيع المعرفة كما تقول نظرية القرآن الكريم .
Indeed, the heavenly scriptures are in need of explanation of their meanings, clarification, and uncovering of what is intended from them, and the prophets used to undertake this role and bestow upon people additional information in various aspects of life, so they formed a source of knowledge as the Quran's theory says. Indeed, the jurists and exegetes say that the heavenly scriptures and the messengers are like an external prophet, and the insights of the soul and its axiomatic constants are like an internal prophet. The conclusion: that the prophets and the heavenly scriptures are two springs from the springs of knowledge as the theory of the Noble Quran says.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines the role of prophets and scriptures as sources of knowledge in the Quranic worldview. The author states that scriptures require explanation, which prophets provide, thereby becoming a source of knowledge. Then, the author reports a view attributed to jurists and exegetes: scriptures and messengers are an 'external prophet,' while innate insights and self-evident truths are an 'internal prophet.' The conclusion combines both as two springs of knowledge. This passage establishes a dual epistemology: external revelation and internal reason/intuition.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic theory of knowledge recognizes both external prophetic guidance and internal rational intuition as complementary springs of knowledge.

Extraordinary Witnessing Knowledge: A Quranic Epistemology
Author introduces a special intuitive knowledge granted to the pious through divine care.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of extraordinary witnessing knowledge as presented in this passage and explain its relation to piety and divine care.

Core Idea
Extraordinary witnessing knowledge is a divine gift to the pious, not a product of human reasoning.

This passage introduces a special category of knowledge that transcends ordinary human acquisition. The author grounds it firmly in Quranic revelation, presenting it as a direct result of divine care for the pious. Understanding this concept is crucial for a complete Islamic epistemology, where knowledge is not only earned through effort but also bestowed through grace.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Read the passage and note who is the recipient of this knowledge. The text explicitly mentions 'the pious person' (al-muttaqī).
2
Extract the prerequisites
What qualities does the person possess? The passage says 'adorned with virtues, free from vices' (al-mutaḥallī bi-l-faḍāʾil al-mutanazzih ʿan al-radhāʾil). This indicates moral and spiritual purification.
3
Identify the process
How is this knowledge acquired? It is not through human effort alone but through divine care (ʿināya), resulting in successive guidance and knowledge (al-hidāya baʿda al-hidāya wa-l-ʿilm baʿda al-ʿilm).
4
Determine the endpoint
What is the ultimate station reached? The passage concludes with 'the station of witnessing' (shuhūd). This is the highest level of knowledge in this context.
The passage is cut off, but shuhūd likely refers to direct, intuitive apprehension of truth.
5
Recognize the source
The author claims that this concept is supported by many Quranic verses. Reflect on how revelation serves as the epistemological foundation for this category.

Source Text

This passage introduces a type of knowledge termed 'extraordinary witnessing knowledge' (al-maʿrifa al-shuhūdiyya al-khāriqa). The author asserts that the Quran contains many verses stating that a pious person who embodies virtues and avoids vices receives divine care. This results in successive guidance and knowledge, allowing him to ascend levels of knowledge until he attains the station of 'witnessing' (shuhūd), which likely refers to a direct, intuitive apprehension of truth. The passage is cut off, so the full discussion is not available, but the idea is that this is a superior form of knowledge granted by God to the righteous.

The Three Levels of Certainty (Yaqīn) in the Quran
Three levels of yaqīn explained with Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Quranic levels of yaqīn (ʿilm al-yaqīn, ʿayn al-yaqīn, ḥaqq al-yaqīn) and explain their fruits.

Core Idea
Certainty in the Quran has three ascending levels: knowledge, witnessing, and experiential truth.

In the Quran, certainty (yaqīn) is not a single state but a journey from intellectual conviction to direct vision to existential realization. This lesson unpacks the three levels mentioned in Surah al-Takāthur and al-Wāqiʿa, showing how each level corresponds to a deeper engagement with the truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
Find ʿilm al-yaqīn, ʿayn al-yaqīn, and ḥaqq al-yaqīn in the Arabic passage. Note their order.
2
Understand the first level
ʿIlm al-yaqīn is knowledge derived from evidence and reasoning. It leads to conviction but not direct vision. The passage says: 'كلا لو تعلمون علم اليقين. لترون الجحيم' – if you had knowledge of certainty, you would see Hellfire.
3
Understand the second level
ʿAyn al-yaqīn is direct visual witnessing. The passage says: 'ثم لترونها عين اليقين' – then you will see it with the eye of certainty.
4
Understand the third level
Ḥaqq al-yaqīn is experiential truth, where the reality is directly apprehended. The passage says: 'إن هذا لهو حق اليقين' – indeed, this is the truth of certainty.
5
Apply the distinction
The author clarifies that seeing Hellfire on the Day of Judgment is common to all, but the higher levels are granted in this life to the righteous based on their merit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغيب ويعطى قدرات خارقة تكريما من الله سبحانه لعباده المخلصين وفيما يأتي بعض هذه الآيات قال تعالى (يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنْ تَتَّقُوا اللَّهَ يَجْعَلْ لَكُمْ فُرْقَانًا) أي يجعل لكم في قلوبكم نورا خاصا تفرقون به بين الصالح والطالح تارة بالبرهنة والاستدلال واخرى بالشهود والمكاشفة وقد تصل العناية الالهية لمن اخلص لله الى مراحل متقدمة تتمثل في صرف السوء والفحشاء عنه فهذا رجل (رأى بُرْهَانَ رَبِّهِ كَذَلِكَ لِنَصْرِفَ عَنْهُ السُّوءَ وَالْفَحْشَاءَ إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصين) وقال تعالى (يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا اتَّقُوا اللَّهَ وَآمِنُوا بِرَسُولِهِ يُؤْتِكُم كِفْلَيْنِ مِنْ رَحْمَتِهِ وَيَجْعَلْ لَكُمْ نُورًا تَمْشُونَ بِهِ) وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ وَيُعَلِّمُكُمُ اللهُ ونلاحظ هنا الصلة الواضحة التي تربط بين التقى والتعليم لاحظ الآية (واعبد رَبِّكَ حَتَّى يَأْتِيَكَ الْيَقينُ) فالعبادة هدف أوسط أما الهدف الاسمى فهو اليقين (اليقين شيء لا يتصور غيره بحال) وبعبارة العلامة الطبطبائي (اليقين: هو اشتداد الإدراك الذهني بحيث لا يقبل الزوال والوهن وهناك ثمار متعددة لمراتب اليقين في المنظور القرآني (كلا لو تعلمون علم اليقين. لترون الجحيم. ثم لترونها عين اليقين) ان الرؤية أو الاطلاع على الجحيم أو رؤية الجنة من ثمرات علم اليقين بالمعارف الالهية وان المشاهدة الاعلى هي (عين اليقين) (ثُمَّ لَتَرُونَهَا عَينَ الْيَقِينِ) ثم مرحلة اعلى تسمى (حق اليقين) قال تعالى إِنْ هَذَا لَهُوَ حَق الْيَقِينِ) والمراد رؤية الجحيم قبل يوم القيامة في هذه الحياة الدنيا لان رؤية الجحيم يوم القيامة أمر ميسور ومتحقق لجميع المؤمنين وجميع الكافرين العالمين منهم والجاهلين. ان هذه الآيات وكثير غيرها تقول إن نافذة الفتوحات الباطنية والمكاشفات والمشاهدات تنزل على الامثل فالامثل من افراد الامة على قدر استحقاقهم وتحملهم. ورد في الحديث الشريف (ومن زهد فيها - في الدنيا فقصر فيها أمله أعطاه الله علما بغير تعلم وهدى بغير هداية وأذهب عنه العماء وجعله بصيرا) وفي الحديث القدسي قول الله عز وجل عن العابد المجاهد (فاذا عشقني وعشقته رفعت الحجب فيما بيني وبينه) (لايزال العبد يتقرب إلي بالنوافل حتى احبه فإذا احببته كنت له سمعا وبصرا وقلبا ويدا ورجلا إن دعاني أجبته وإن ناداني لبيته) وقال عز من قائل (عبدي اطعني حتى اجعلك مثلي اقول للشئ كن فيكون تقول للشيء كن فيكون وفي قراءة موحدة لنصوص هذا الفصل نرى الصور الآتية: الذين آمنوا بالله ورسوله) (واتقوا الله) (وعبدوا الله مخلصين) وكسبوا العلم الرصين العميق) (وزهدوا في الدنيا) (وعشقوا الله بكل جوارحهم سيصل كل منهم الى النتائج الآتية (يجعل له فرقانا) (يؤته كفلين
The unseen and gives extraordinary powers as an honor from God Almighty to His sincere servants. And following are some of these verses: He said the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, if you fear Allah, He will grant you a criterion' meaning He will place in your hearts a special light by which you distinguish between the righteous and the corrupt, sometimes by evidence and reasoning and other times by witnessing and unveiling. And divine care may reach those who are sincere to God to advanced stages represented in turning away evil and immorality from him. So this man 'saw the proof of his Lord. Thus did We turn away from him evil and immorality. Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants.' And He said the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, fear Allah and believe in His Messenger; He will give you a double portion of His mercy and make for you a light by which you walk.' And 'Fear Allah, and Allah will teach you.' And we note here the clear connection linking piety and knowledge. Note the verse: 'And worship your Lord until there comes to you the certainty.' So worship is an intermediate goal, while the ultimate goal is certainty (certainty is something that cannot be conceived otherwise). And in the words of Allamah Tabatabai: 'Certainty is the intensification of mental perception such that it does not accept removal or weakness.' And there are multiple fruits of the ranks of certainty in the Quranic perspective: 'No! If you only knew with knowledge of certainty. You will surely see Hellfire. Then you will surely see it with the eye of certainty.' So the vision or seeing of Hellfire or seeing Paradise is among the fruits of knowledge of certainty with divine cognitions, and the highest witnessing is the 'eye of certainty' ('Then you will surely see it with the eye of certainty'). Then a higher stage called 'truth of certainty' – He said the Exalted: 'Indeed, this is the truth of certainty.' And the intended meaning is seeing Hellfire before the Day of Judgment in this worldly life, because seeing Hellfire on the Day of Judgment is something easy and actualized for all believers and all disbelievers, the knowledgeable among them and the ignorant. These verses and many others say that the window of inner openings, unveilings, and visions descends upon the best of the best of the individuals of the community according to their merit and capacity. It is reported in the noble hadith: 'Whoever abstains from it – from the world – and shortens his hope in it, Allah will give him knowledge without learning and guidance without guidance, and will remove from him blindness and make him seeing.' And in the sacred hadith, the saying of God Almighty about the worshipping mujahid: 'When he loves Me and I love him, I lift the veils between me and him.' 'The servant continues to draw near to Me through supererogatory acts until I love him. And when I love him, I become his hearing, his sight, his heart, his hand, and his foot. If he calls Me, I answer him; if he asks Me, I give him.' And He said, the Exalted from the Speaker: 'My servant, obey Me until I make you like Me: I say to a thing 'Be' and it is, and you say to a thing 'Be' and it is.' And in a unified reading of the texts of this chapter, we see the following images: those who believe in Allah and His Messenger, fear Allah, worship Allah sincerely, acquire deep solid knowledge, abstain from the world, and love Allah with all their limbs – each of them will reach the following results: He will make for him a criterion, He will give him a double portion.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion from a Quranic perspective, focusing on the concept of certainty (yaqīn) and its levels. The author begins by citing verses that link piety (taqwa) with divine gifts such as furqān (criterion) and nūr (light). The verse from Surah al-Anfāl (29) is explained: piety leads to a special light in the heart to distinguish truth from falsehood, both through reasoning (burhān/istidlāl) and through direct witnessing (shuhūd/mukāshafa). The author then mentions the story of Yūsuf (as) where divine care turned away evil from him. The connection between taqwa and knowledge is emphasized with the verse 'Fear Allah, and Allah will teach you' (al-Baqara: 282). Worship is presented as a means, with certainty as the ultimate goal. The author quotes Allamah Tabatabai's definition of yaqīn: the intensification of mental perception such that it is impervious to removal or weakness. The three Quranic levels of certainty are then described: 'ilm al-yaqīn (knowledge of certainty) – seeing Hellfire through intellectual conviction; 'ayn al-yaqīn (eye of certainty) – direct vision of Hellfire; and haqq al-yaqīn (truth of certainty) – experiencing the reality. The author clarifies that seeing Hellfire in this life is a special favor for the righteous, as on the Day of Judgment it will be seen by all. Finally, hadiths are cited about the rewards of zuhd (abstinence) and divine love (ʿishq), culminating in the idea that sincere belief, piety, worship, knowledge, zuhd, and love lead to results such as furqān and a double portion of mercy. The passage emphasizes that spiritual unveiling and visions are granted according to one's merit.

Key Terms
اليقين
علم اليقين
عين اليقين
حق اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fruit of ʿilm al-yaqīn regarding Hellfire?
A) Hearing about Hellfire
B) Seeing Hellfire with the eye of certainty
C) Knowing that Hellfire exists through evidence
D) Experiencing Hellfire directly

Key Takeaway
The Quran describes three escalating levels of certainty: rational knowledge, direct vision, and experiential truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own words, describe the difference between ʿilm al-yaqīn and ʿayn al-yaqīn, using the example of Paradise. How would knowledge of Paradise differ from seeing it?
?
Find another verse in the Quran that mentions the word yaqīn or one of its derivatives and classify which level it refers to.

The Criterion (Furqān) as a Divine Light for Distinguishing Truth
The concept of furqān as a gift for the pious.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of furqān as a special light granted to the pious to distinguish truth from falsehood through reasoning and unveiling.

Core Idea
Furqān is an inner light from taqwa that enables both rational discernment and spiritual vision.

The Quran promises that those who fear Allah will receive a 'furqān' – a criterion to discriminate truth from falsehood. This lesson explores how this furqān functions both as an intellectual tool (through evidence) and as a spiritual faculty (through unveiling), as explained in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Locate the verse
Find the verse 'يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنْ تَتَّقُوا اللَّهَ يَجْعَلْ لَكُمْ فُرْقَانًا' in the Arabic. It connects piety with receiving a criterion.
2
Understand the explanation
The author says the furqān is a special light in the heart that distinguishes between the righteous and the corrupt.
3
Identify the two modes
The author mentions two ways this furqān works: 'تارة بالبرهنة والاستدلال واخرى بالشهود والمكاشفة' – sometimes through evidence and reasoning, other times through witnessing and unveiling.
4
See the fruit
The passage then mentions that divine care can reach advanced stages where evil is turned away, as in the story of Yusuf (AS) who saw the proof of his Lord.
5
Connect to knowledge
The author notes the link between taqwa and knowledge, citing 'وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ وَيُعَلِّمُكُمُ اللهُ' – fear Allah and Allah will teach you.

The unseen and gives extraordinary powers as an honor from God Almighty to His sincere servants. And following are some of these verses: He said the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, if you fear Allah, He will grant you a criterion' meaning He will place in your hearts a special light by which you distinguish between the righteous and the corrupt, sometimes by evidence and reasoning and other times by witnessing and unveiling. And divine care may reach those who are sincere to God to advanced stages represented in turning away evil and immorality from him. So this man 'saw the proof of his Lord. Thus did We turn away from him evil and immorality. Indeed, he was of Our chosen servants.' And He said the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, fear Allah and believe in His Messenger; He will give you a double portion of His mercy and make for you a light by which you walk.' And 'Fear Allah, and Allah will teach you.' And we note here the clear connection linking piety and knowledge. Note the verse: 'And worship your Lord until there comes to you the certainty.' So worship is an intermediate goal, while the ultimate goal is certainty (certainty is something that cannot be conceived otherwise). And in the words of Allamah Tabatabai: 'Certainty is the intensification of mental perception such that it does not accept removal or weakness.' And there are multiple fruits of the ranks of certainty in the Quranic perspective: 'No! If you only knew with knowledge of certainty. You will surely see Hellfire. Then you will surely see it with the eye of certainty.' So the vision or seeing of Hellfire or seeing Paradise is among the fruits of knowledge of certainty with divine cognitions, and the highest witnessing is the 'eye of certainty' ('Then you will surely see it with the eye of certainty'). Then a higher stage called 'truth of certainty' – He said the Exalted: 'Indeed, this is the truth of certainty.' And the intended meaning is seeing Hellfire before the Day of Judgment in this worldly life, because seeing Hellfire on the Day of Judgment is something easy and actualized for all believers and all disbelievers, the knowledgeable among them and the ignorant. These verses and many others say that the window of inner openings, unveilings, and visions descends upon the best of the best of the individuals of the community according to their merit and capacity. It is reported in the noble hadith: 'Whoever abstains from it – from the world – and shortens his hope in it, Allah will give him knowledge without learning and guidance without guidance, and will remove from him blindness and make him seeing.' And in the sacred hadith, the saying of God Almighty about the worshipping mujahid: 'When he loves Me and I love him, I lift the veils between me and him.' 'The servant continues to draw near to Me through supererogatory acts until I love him. And when I love him, I become his hearing, his sight, his heart, his hand, and his foot. If he calls Me, I answer him; if he asks Me, I give him.' And He said, the Exalted from the Speaker: 'My servant, obey Me until I make you like Me: I say to a thing 'Be' and it is, and you say to a thing 'Be' and it is.' And in a unified reading of the texts of this chapter, we see the following images: those who believe in Allah and His Messenger, fear Allah, worship Allah sincerely, acquire deep solid knowledge, abstain from the world, and love Allah with all their limbs – each of them will reach the following results: He will make for him a criterion, He will give him a double portion.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion from a Quranic perspective, focusing on the concept of certainty (yaqīn) and its levels. The author begins by citing verses that link piety (taqwa) with divine gifts such as furqān (criterion) and nūr (light). The verse from Surah al-Anfāl (29) is explained: piety leads to a special light in the heart to distinguish truth from falsehood, both through reasoning (burhān/istidlāl) and through direct witnessing (shuhūd/mukāshafa). The author then mentions the story of Yūsuf (as) where divine care turned away evil from him. The connection between taqwa and knowledge is emphasized with the verse 'Fear Allah, and Allah will teach you' (al-Baqara: 282). Worship is presented as a means, with certainty as the ultimate goal. The author quotes Allamah Tabatabai's definition of yaqīn: the intensification of mental perception such that it is impervious to removal or weakness. The three Quranic levels of certainty are then described: 'ilm al-yaqīn (knowledge of certainty) – seeing Hellfire through intellectual conviction; 'ayn al-yaqīn (eye of certainty) – direct vision of Hellfire; and haqq al-yaqīn (truth of certainty) – experiencing the reality. The author clarifies that seeing Hellfire in this life is a special favor for the righteous, as on the Day of Judgment it will be seen by all. Finally, hadiths are cited about the rewards of zuhd (abstinence) and divine love (ʿishq), culminating in the idea that sincere belief, piety, worship, knowledge, zuhd, and love lead to results such as furqān and a double portion of mercy. The passage emphasizes that spiritual unveiling and visions are granted according to one's merit.

البرهنة
الاستدلال
الشهود
المكاشفة

Stages of the Spiritual Journey (Manazil al-Sa'irin)
Author lists the stages (manazil) that a gnostic traverses to attain special knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten stages of the spiritual journey (manazil al-sa'irin) and explain the epistemological significance of the final stage (muhaddath).

Core Idea
The ten stations transform the seeker from veiled to illuminated, culminating in being spoken to by angels.

The Islamic gnostics describe a structured path for attaining special knowledge. This path consists of ten stages that purify the soul and lift the veils, leading to certain knowledge and the station of muhaddath. In this lesson, we will walk through each stage and understand how they function epistemologically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first stage (yaqaza)
Wakefulness is the initial awakening from heedlessness. It is the turning point that starts the journey.
In the passage, yaqaza is listed first.
2
Trace the ethical purification stages
Note the sequence: repentance, self-accounting, turning back, reflection, remembrance, holding fast, separation, reining in the soul. These represent moral and spiritual cleansing.
Stages 2-9 are ethical and preparatory.
3
Understand the culmination – perceiving the subtleties (dirak al-lataif)
The tenth stage is the direct perception of spiritual realities after the veils are lifted. This leads to strong certainty.
The passage says when veils are lifted, the soul sees the world of mulk and shahada.
4
Recognize the final station (muhaddath)
The gnostic who reaches this level is called muhaddath – one spoken to by angels. This indicates a direct, inspired knowledge beyond ordinary learning.
The term muhaddath is from hadith; here it is applied to the perfected gnostic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما حدد العرفاء الاسلاميون آلية الاشراق وخرق الحجب فقالوا ان العارف اذا اراد الوصول الى المعرفة الخاصة طوى المراحل اللازمة . التي تسمى عندهم منازل السائرين وهي -1 اليقظة -۲ التوبة -٣- المحاسبة -٤ الإنابة -٥ التفكر -٦ التذكر -٧ الإعتصام -^ الانقطاع -۹ كبح جماح النفس -1. درك اللطائف فإذا ارتفعت حجب العصبية والسيئات عن النفس تجلت لها صورة عالم الملك والشهادة بحسب ما تستطيعه من الاخذ والتلقي على قدر الدين تكون قوة اليقين وعندها يلقب العارف بانه (محدث) قال الامام الصادق السلام ( العارف شخصه مع الخلق
Just as the Islamic gnostics defined the mechanism of illumination and breaking the veils, saying that when the gnostic wants to attain special knowledge, he traverses the necessary stages, which are called in their terminology 'the stations of the travelers' (manazil al-sa'irin), namely: 1. Wakefulness (yaqaza), 2. Repentance (tawba), 3. Self-accounting (muhasaba), 4. Turning back (inaba), 5. Reflection (tafakkur), 6. Remembrance (tadhakkur), 7. Holding fast (i'tisam), 8. Separation (inqita'), 9. Reining in the soul (kabh jamaah al-nafs), 10. Perceiving the subtleties (dirak al-lataif). So when the veils of partisanship and evil deeds are lifted from the soul, the form of the world of kingdom and testimony becomes manifest to it according to its capacity to take and receive, proportional to religion; then the strength of certainty is attained, and at that time the gnostic is called 'muhaddath' (one who is spoken to by angels). Imam al-Sadiq, peace be upon him, said: 'The gnostic, his person is with the creation...'
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage describes the practical methodology of spiritual realization according to Islamic gnostics. The author presents the 'stations of the travelers' (manazil al-sa'irin), a sequence of ten stages that a seeker (salik) must traverse to attain special knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-khassa). The stages begin with wakefulness (yaqaza), the initial awakening to spiritual reality, followed by repentance (tawba), self-accounting (muhasaba), turning back to God (inaba), reflection (tafakkur), remembrance (tadhakkur), holding fast to God (i'tisam), separation from worldly attachments (inqita'), reining in the ego (kabh jamaah al-nafs), and finally perceiving the subtleties (dirak al-lataif). The author then explains that when the veils of partisanship and evil deeds are lifted, the soul sees the world of mulk (kingdom) and shahada (testimony) in proportion to its capacity, leading to strong certainty (qawwat al-yaqin). At this stage, the gnostic is called 'muhaddath' (one spoken to by angels), a term occurring in hadith. The passage ends with a quote from Imam al-Sadiq, emphasizing that the gnostic is with the creation outwardly while his heart is with the Creator.

اليقظة
محدث

Intellect and Senses Are the Foundation of All Knowledge
Author defends role of intellect and senses against gnostic depreciation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's argument that intellect and senses are the necessary foundation even for supernormal knowledge, and support it with the Quranic example of Solomon's throne.

Core Idea
Intellect and senses are the foundation, not a hindrance, for all knowledge.

Some mystics claim that true knowledge comes only through direct divine illumination, dismissing intellect and senses as inferior. The author rejects this view, arguing that even supernormal knowledge relies on these faculties. To prove his point, he recalls the Quranic story of Prophet Solomon's request to bring the throne of Bilqis, showing that even extraordinary feats require a foundation in rational and sensory perception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the gnostic position
Find where the author presents the gnostic analogy (like walking with a wooden leg). What is the gnostic claim about those who rely on intellect and senses?
The analogy belittles rational and sensory knowledge compared to mystical insight.
2
Extract the author's rebuttal
Look for the author's direct response: 'the reality is that intellect and senses are the station of perception even in supernormal knowledge.' Note that he asserts they are foundational, not optional.
The word 'muḥaṭṭa' (station) implies a necessary starting point.
3
Analyze the scriptural evidence
Read the Quranic account of Solomon's throne. Notice the two different characters: a jinn (ifrit) and one with 'knowledge from the Book'. Who succeeded? How does this illustrate the author's point?
The one with knowledge from the Book succeeds instantly, showing that such knowledge is an extension of intellect, not a rejection.
4
Connect the story to the thesis
Explain how the example supports the author's claim. The extraordinary power (bringing the throne in an instant) came from possessing part of ‘ilm al-kitāb, which itself is based on intellect and revelation, not from abandoning reason.
The author implies that supernormal abilities are a gift from God that build upon, rather than replace, natural faculties.
5
Formulate the conclusion
Summarize the author's argument: Intellect and senses are indispensable for all knowledge, including mystical and divine knowledge. Any claim to the contrary misunderstands their role.
This conclusion defends the harmony between rational inquiry and spiritual insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن بعض العرفانيين يشبهون من يتبع المعرفة بالعقل والحس بمن يمشي برجل خشبية اذا ما قيس بالعارف ولكن الواقع ان العقل والحس هما محطة الادراك حتى في المعرفة الخارقة أو الاشراقية أو الالهية. وفي قصة نبي الله سليمان عليه السلام لما أراد جلب عرش الملكة بلقيس من اليمن الى دمشق وضوح في مدى القدرة الخارقة التي يحصل عليها من لديه جزء من علم الكتاب قال تعالى (قَالَ يَا أَيُّهَا الْمَلَأُ أَيُّكُمْ يَأْتِينِي بِعَرشِهَا قَبل أن يَأْتُونِي مُسلِمِينَ . قَالَ عِفْريت من الْجِنِّ أَنَا أَتيك به قبل أن تقوم من مقامكَ وَإِنِّي عَلَيْهِ لَقَوِي أَمِينٌ ، قَالَ الَّذِي عِندَهُ عِلْمٌ مِنَ الْكِتَابِ أَنَا آتِيكَ بِهِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يَرتَدَّ إِلَيْكَ طَرْفُكَ فَلَمَا رَأَهُ مُسْتَقِرًا عِنْدَهُ قَالَ هَذَا مِنْ فَضْلِ رَبِّي)
Indeed, some of the gnostics liken one who follows knowledge through intellect and senses to one who walks with a wooden leg when compared to the knower. But the reality is that intellect and senses are the station of perception even in supernormal, illuminative, or divine knowledge. And in the story of Prophet Solomon, peace be upon him, when he wanted to bring the throne of the Queen of Sheba from Yemen to Damascus, there is clarity in the extent of the supernormal power attained by one who has a part of the knowledge of the Book. He said, exalted: 'He said, O assembly, which of you will bring me her throne before they come to me in submission? A strong one from the jinn said, I will bring it to you before you rise from your station, and indeed I am strong and trustworthy. He who had knowledge from the Book said, I will bring it to you before your glance returns to you.' And when he saw it settled before him, he said, 'This is from the favor of my Lord.'
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage engages with a specific gnostic tendency to depreciate rational and sensory knowledge. The author begins by presenting the gnostic analogy: one who relies on intellect and senses is like a person walking with a wooden leg compared to a true knower. The author immediately rejects this, asserting that intellect and senses are actually the very station (muḥaṭṭa) of perception—even for supernormal, illuminative, or divine knowledge. He then supports his point with the Quranic narrative of Solomon's request for the throne of Bilqis. Two responses are offered: a jinn (ifrit) offers to bring it before Solomon rises, but one who had 'knowledge from the Book' (identified by many commentators as Asif ibn Barkhiya) brings it in the blink of an eye. The author emphasizes that this extraordinary power derived from possessing a part of the knowledge of the Book—yet it did not bypass intellect and senses; rather, they were the foundation. The passage thus defends the integral role of rational and sensory faculties even in the highest forms of mystical knowledge, using a scriptural example to illustrate that supernormal abilities are not a rejection of these faculties but an extension of them through divine favor.

Key Terms
العرفانيون
محطة الادراك
علم الكتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They are replaced entirely by divine illumination.

Key Takeaway
Intellect and senses are not barriers to higher knowledge but are essential foundations that even supernormal abilities build upon.

Imam Ali's Claim to Comprehensive Knowledge
Author cites Imam Ali's claim to comprehensive knowledge and his description of worship as love.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Imam Ali's statement about his knowledge of the paths of heaven and earth to distinguish between empirical and spiritual knowledge.

Core Idea
Spiritual knowledge encompasses both the seen and the unseen.

In this passage, Imam Ali makes a bold epistemological claim: he is more knowledgeable of the ways of heaven than the ways of earth. This is not a literal map of the sky but a declaration of insight into divine realities. Understanding this claim helps us appreciate the Islamic concept of 'ilm (knowledge) that goes beyond empirical observation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Read the first part: 'فَلَأَنَا بِطُرُقِ السَّمَاءِ أَعْلَمُ مِنِّي بِطُرُقِ الْأَرْضِ'. What exactly is Imam Ali asserting about his knowledge?
He claims superiority in knowledge of celestial (spiritual) matters over terrestrial (worldly) matters.
2
Contrast Celestial and Terrestrial Knowledge
Consider the two domains: 'السماء' (heaven) and 'الأرض' (earth). What kind of knowledge belongs to each? Is one empirical and the other revealed?
Celestial knowledge here refers to spiritual and metaphysical truths; terrestrial knowledge refers to worldly affairs.
3
Assess the Epistemological Implication
If Imam Ali is more knowledgeable in spiritual matters, what does that imply about the source of his knowledge? How does this relate to his status as an Imam?
It implies a divinely bestowed knowledge (علم لدني) that is superior to empirical learning.
4
Reflect on Modern Relevance
How does this claim challenge purely empirical epistemologies that deny knowledge of the unseen?

Source Text

قال ايها الناس سلوني قبل ان تفقدوني فلأنا بطرق السماء اعلم مني بطرق الارض) ربي ما عبدتك خوفا من نارك ، ولا طمعا في جنتك ولكن وجدتك أهلا للعبادة فعبدتك) وهذه تسمى عبادة العاشقين .
He said: O people, ask me before you lose me, for I am more knowledgeable of the paths of the heavens than I am of the paths of the earth. My Lord, I did not worship You out of fear of Your fire, nor out of desire for Your Paradise, but I found You worthy of worship, so I worshipped You. And this is called the worship of lovers.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents a statement attributed to Imam Ali (عليه السلام) regarding his comprehensive knowledge and his unique form of worship. The passage is epistemological as it distinguishes between different motivations for worship. Imam Ali claims knowledge of both celestial and terrestrial paths, indicating a deep spiritual insight. Then he defines his worship as purely motivated by love for God, not fear of hell or desire for paradise. This is termed 'عبادة العاشقين' (worship of lovers). The text implies that such worship is superior and stems from advanced spiritual knowledge.

The Three Motivations for Worship
Author cites Imam Ali's claim to comprehensive knowledge and his description of worship as love.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of worship motivation (fear, hope, love) and explain why love-based worship is considered superior.

Core Idea
True worship is motivated by love for God, not by fear of punishment or desire for reward.

Imam Ali distinguishes his worship from that of those who act out of fear of hell or hope for paradise. He states that he worships God solely because He is worthy of worship. This third motivation is called 'عبادة العاشقين' (worship of lovers). Understanding these categories helps purify one's intention (ikhlas) and achieve a higher spiritual station.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Motivations
Read the part: 'ما عبدتك خوفا من نارك ، ولا طمعا في جنتك ولكن وجدتك أهلا للعبادة فعبدتك'. What are the three motivations mentioned?
Fear of fire (خوفا من نارك), hope for paradise (طمعا في جنتك), and love because God is worthy (وجدتك أهلا للعبادة).
2
Extract the Definition
What is 'عبادة العاشقين' according to the text? How does it differ from the other two?
It is worship motivated purely by love, without consideration of reward or punishment.
3
Compare with Other Types
In Islamic spirituality, these three correspond to the worship of slaves (fear), merchants (hope), and free men (love). How does each affect one's relationship with God?
Fear-based worship is uneasy, hope-based is transactional, love-based is intimate and sincere.
4
Evaluate the Superiority Claim
Why is love-based worship considered superior? Refer to the text's implication that it comes from 'higher' knowledge.
It reflects a deeper recognition of God's attributes and a more selfless devotion.

Epistemic Tools of the Islamic Gnostic vs Sufi Gnostic
Author distinguishes Islamic gnostic from Sufi gnostic by breadth of epistemic tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Islamic gnostic and the Sufi gnostic based on the breadth of their epistemic tools.

Core Idea
The Islamic gnostic uses five epistemic tools; the Sufi gnostic limits himself to inspiration.

In this passage, the author first recounts the story of Prophet Yusuf to show that divine knowledge can be accompanied by extraordinary physical means. He then draws a sharp epistemological contrast: the Sufi gnostic restricts knowledge to inspiration (ilham), whereas the Islamic gnostic accepts multiple instruments—the senses, inspiration, reason, the heart, and revelation. This distinction matters because it determines which sources of knowledge are considered valid in seeking truth about God and reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate the terms 'العارف الإسلامي' and 'العارف الصوفي' in the passage. Note the author's explicit statement of difference.
2
List the epistemic tools
From the passage, extract the five tools mentioned: الحس (senses), الإلهام (inspiration), العقل (reason), القلب (heart), الوحي (revelation).
3
Compare the scope
Which tools does the Sufi accept? Which does the Islamic gnostic accept? The key difference is that the Sufi restricts to inspiration, while the Islamic gnostic uses all five.
4
Apply to the Yusuf example
How does the story of Yusuf illustrate multiple tools? The shirt represents a physical means (senses/empirical), the command to go to Egypt shows reason, and the knowledge 'ilm is from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونلاحظ الأمر نفسه مع نبي الله يوسف عليه السلام فلما بلغ أشده أتيناه حكما وعلما وكذلك نجزي المحسنين . ونلاحظ أيضا أنه بعدما امتلك العلم والحكمة امتلك القدرة الخارقة فكان قميصه وسيلة لعلاج أبيه من العمى . قال لا تثريب عليكم اليوم يغفر الله لكم وهو أرحم الراحمين . اذهبوا بقميصي هذا فألقوه على وجه أبي يأت بصيرا وأتوني بأهلكم أجمعين . فلما أن جاء البشير ألقاه على وجهه فارتد بصيرا . إن الفرق بين العارف الإسلامي والعارف الصوفي هو الآتي (۱) أن الصوفي يحصر أدوات المعرفة بالإلهام في حين أن العارف الإسلامي يؤمن بتعدد أدوات المعرفة - الحس والإلهام والعقل والقلب والوحي الخ .
And we observe the same matter with the prophet of God Yusuf (peace be upon him). When he reached his maturity, We gave him judgment and knowledge, and thus do We reward the doers of good. And we also observe that after he possessed knowledge and wisdom, he possessed extraordinary power, so his shirt became a means to cure his father from blindness. He said: No blame on you today; may God forgive you, and He is the Most Merciful of the merciful. Go with my shirt and cast it over my father's face, he will become seeing, and bring me your entire family. Then when the bearer of good news came, he cast it over his face, and he returned seeing. Indeed, the difference between the Islamic gnostic and the Sufi gnostic is as follows: (1) The Sufi restricts the instruments of knowledge to inspiration, while the Islamic gnostic believes in multiple instruments of knowledge – the senses, inspiration, reason, the heart, and revelation, etc.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage first presents the example of Prophet Yusuf (عليه السلام) to illustrate that knowledge and wisdom from God are accompanied by extraordinary abilities (kharq al-ada). Yusuf's shirt, a physical object, becomes a means of restoring his father's sight, demonstrating that knowledge (ilm) and wisdom (hikma) from divine sources can produce miraculous effects. The author uses this as a prelude to defining the difference between two types of gnostics. The Sufi gnostic is characterized as restricting the means of knowledge to inspiration (ilham) alone, whereas the Islamic gnostic (al-`arif al-islami) acknowledges multiple means: senses (hiss), inspiration (ilham), reason (aql), heart (qalb), and revelation (wahy). This distinction is epistemological: it concerns the sources and tools of knowledge. The Islamic gnostic does not reject inspiration but does not limit knowledge to it; he accepts empirical, rational, and spiritual avenues. The example of Yusuf supports this by showing that knowledge can manifest through physical objects (the shirt) and through reason (the command to go to Egypt), implying multiple instruments.

Key Terms
العارف الإسلامي
العارف الصوفي
أدوات المعرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary difference between the Islamic gnostic and the Sufi gnostic?
A) The Sufi uses reason while the Islamic gnostic uses inspiration
B) The Islamic gnostic uses multiple knowledge instruments while the Sufi restricts to inspiration
C) The Islamic gnostic rejects inspiration entirely
D) The Sufi gnostic uses both reason and inspiration equally

Key Takeaway
The Islamic gnostic's epistemology is inclusive of all legitimate sources of knowledge, while the Sufi gnostic's is exclusive to inspiration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Using the Yusuf account, identify which epistemic tools are demonstrated in the episode of the shirt. Explain how the shirt's role combines physical causality (senses) with divine wisdom (revelation).

Epistemological Tools: Sufi vs Islamic Gnostic
First difference: tools of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Sufi's restriction of knowledge to inspiration and the Islamic gnostic's acceptance of multiple tools.

Core Idea
The Islamic gnostic uses a broader epistemic toolkit than the Sufi.

This lesson focuses on the first difference the author highlights: the scope of the tools of knowledge. Understanding this distinction is foundational for evaluating claims of spiritual knowledge in Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sufi position
Locate in the passage where the Sufi's view on the tools of knowledge is stated. What single tool does the Sufi rely on?
2
Identify the Islamic gnostic position
Find the list of tools the Islamic gnostic accepts. Note the variety mentioned.
The passage lists: sense, inspiration, intellect, heart, revelation.
3
Compare the scope
Contrast the Sufi's single tool with the plural tools of the Islamic gnostic. What does this imply about each approach's epistemological foundation?
4
Assess implications for Islamic epistemology
Consider how each view aligns with the Islamic emphasis on multiple sources of knowledge (Quran, Sunnah, reason, etc.). Which is more comprehensive?
Refer to Usul al-Fiqh for classical sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان الفرق بين العارف الاسلامي والصوفي هو الآتي (۱) ان الصوفي يحصر أدوات المعرفة بالالهام في حين ان العارف الاسلامي يؤمن بتعدد أدوات المعرفة - الحس والالهام والعقل والقلب والوحي الخ ۲ ) الصوفي يقول ان التمارين والرقصات والاناشيد والمكابدات تؤدي الى المعرفة والكشف في حين يقول العارف الاسلامي ان الالتزام بالعبادات كلها وقواعد الشريعة ظاهرها وباطنها يؤدي الى المعرفة والكشف. ۳) ان الصوفي يقول انه يصل الى مرحلة الاتحاد بالذات الالهية في حين يقول العارف الاسلامي انه في جميع الاحوال والمعارف والدرجات يبقى يتلقى المعرفة من الفيض الالهي والتكريم الالهي تكريم السيد للعبد . الخلاصة : ان الانسان إذا ارتقى في سلم الطهارات والعبادات والعلم يكرمه الله بمعرفة غير طبيعية تخترق الحجب الغائبة عن حواسنا فيرى اشياء غيبية من عالم الملكوت وقد يكرمه الله تعالى بان يسخر له بعض الجن او بعض الملائكة يطيعونه فيما يريد.
The difference between the Islamic gnostic and the Sufi is as follows: (1) The Sufi restricts the tools of knowledge to inspiration, while the Islamic gnostic believes in multiple tools of knowledge — sense, inspiration, intellect, heart, revelation, etc. (2) The Sufi says that exercises, dances, chants, and hardships lead to knowledge and unveiling, while the Islamic gnostic says that adherence to all acts of worship and the rules of the Sharia, both outward and inward, leads to knowledge and unveiling. (3) The Sufi says that he reaches the stage of union with the divine essence, while the Islamic gnostic says that in all circumstances, knowledges, and stations, he continues to receive knowledge from divine emanation and divine honoring, the honoring of the master by the servant. Conclusion: When a person ascends the ladder of purifications, acts of worship, and knowledge, God honors him with a supernatural knowledge that penetrates the veils hidden from our senses, so he sees unseen things from the world of the kingdom, and God may honor him by subjugating to him some jinn or some angels who obey him in what he wills.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical comparison between two epistemological positions within Islamic spirituality: the Islamic ‘arif (gnostic) and the Sufi. The author presents three distinct points of difference. First, regarding the sources of knowledge: the Sufi limits knowledge to inspiration (ilham), whereas the Islamic gnostic admits a plurality of tools including sense perception, inspiration, reason, heart, and revelation. Second, regarding the method of attaining knowledge: the Sufi relies on physical exercises, dances, chants, and hardships, while the Islamic gnostic emphasizes full compliance with the outward and inward rules of Sharia. Third, regarding the ultimate goal: the Sufi claims to reach union (ittihad) with the divine essence, while the Islamic gnostic maintains that he remains a recipient of divine emanation (fayd) and honor, analogous to a master honoring a servant. The passage concludes with a summary: as a person ascends in purification, worship, and knowledge, God grants him supernatural knowledge that penetrates the unseen, and may even subject jinn or angels to his will. The author does not present a logical argument but rather an exposition of contrasting views.

Key Terms
أدوات المعرفة
العارف الإسلامي
الصوفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary difference between the Sufi and the Islamic gnostic regarding the tools of knowledge?
A) The Sufi uses reason while the Islamic gnostic uses inspiration.
B) The Sufi restricts knowledge to inspiration while the Islamic gnostic uses multiple tools including sense, intellect, and revelation.
C) Both rely solely on revelation.
D) The Islamic gnostic rejects all tools except the heart.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic gnostic's epistemology is pluralistic, incorporating sensory, rational, and revealed sources, unlike the Sufi's exclusive reliance on inspiration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the tools of knowledge mentioned for the Islamic gnostic and compare them to the classical sources of knowledge in Usul al-Fiqh.

Method of Attaining Knowledge: Exercises vs Sharia
Second difference: method to attain knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Sufi reliance on physical exercises and chants with the Islamic gnostic's emphasis on full Sharia compliance.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes through disciplined worship, not antinomian practices.

The second difference concerns the method by which one attains spiritual knowledge and unveiling. This lesson unpacks how each tradition approaches the path to gnosis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sufi method
Find the specific practices the Sufi employs: exercises, dances, chants, and hardships.
2
Identify the Islamic gnostic method
Note the alternative: adherence to all acts of worship and Sharia rules, both outward and inward.
3
Analyze the contrast
Compare the two approaches: one is extra-Sharia and performative, the other is within the bounds of Islamic law.
4
Evaluate alignment with Islamic principles
Which method is more consistent with the Quranic emphasis on 'ibadah and following the Prophet? Consider potential innovation (bid'ah) in the Sufi practices.
