Ultimate Goal: Union with God vs Divine Emanation
Third difference: ultimate goal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the Sufi claim of union with the divine essence and the Islamic gnostic's concept of receiving divine emanation as a servant.

Core Idea
The Islamic gnostic maintains the Creator-creature distinction, rejecting pantheistic union.

The third difference addresses the ultimate spiritual goal. This lesson examines the theological line between mystical union and the orthodox relationship of servant and Master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sufi claim
Find the phrase where the Sufi says he reaches 'union with the divine essence'.
2
Identify the Islamic gnostic claim
Note how the Islamic gnostic describes his state: receiving knowledge from divine emanation (fayd) and honor (takrim), like a master honoring a servant.
3
Compare the relationship with God
In the Sufi view, the human becomes one with God; in the Islamic gnostic view, the human remains distinct and dependent.
4
Assess theological implications
Which view better preserves tawhid? How does the Islamic gnostic's position align with Ash'ari theology on the attributes of God?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان الفرق بين العارف الاسلامي والصوفي هو الآتي (۱) ان الصوفي يحصر أدوات المعرفة بالالهام في حين ان العارف الاسلامي يؤمن بتعدد أدوات المعرفة - الحس والالهام والعقل والقلب والوحي الخ ۲ ) الصوفي يقول ان التمارين والرقصات والاناشيد والمكابدات تؤدي الى المعرفة والكشف في حين يقول العارف الاسلامي ان الالتزام بالعبادات كلها وقواعد الشريعة ظاهرها وباطنها يؤدي الى المعرفة والكشف. ۳) ان الصوفي يقول انه يصل الى مرحلة الاتحاد بالذات الالهية في حين يقول العارف الاسلامي انه في جميع الاحوال والمعارف والدرجات يبقى يتلقى المعرفة من الفيض الالهي والتكريم الالهي تكريم السيد للعبد . الخلاصة : ان الانسان إذا ارتقى في سلم الطهارات والعبادات والعلم يكرمه الله بمعرفة غير طبيعية تخترق الحجب الغائبة عن حواسنا فيرى اشياء غيبية من عالم الملكوت وقد يكرمه الله تعالى بان يسخر له بعض الجن او بعض الملائكة يطيعونه فيما يريد.
The difference between the Islamic gnostic and the Sufi is as follows: (1) The Sufi restricts the tools of knowledge to inspiration, while the Islamic gnostic believes in multiple tools of knowledge — sense, inspiration, intellect, heart, revelation, etc. (2) The Sufi says that exercises, dances, chants, and hardships lead to knowledge and unveiling, while the Islamic gnostic says that adherence to all acts of worship and the rules of the Sharia, both outward and inward, leads to knowledge and unveiling. (3) The Sufi says that he reaches the stage of union with the divine essence, while the Islamic gnostic says that in all circumstances, knowledges, and stations, he continues to receive knowledge from divine emanation and divine honoring, the honoring of the master by the servant. Conclusion: When a person ascends the ladder of purifications, acts of worship, and knowledge, God honors him with a supernatural knowledge that penetrates the veils hidden from our senses, so he sees unseen things from the world of the kingdom, and God may honor him by subjugating to him some jinn or some angels who obey him in what he wills.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical comparison between two epistemological positions within Islamic spirituality: the Islamic ‘arif (gnostic) and the Sufi. The author presents three distinct points of difference. First, regarding the sources of knowledge: the Sufi limits knowledge to inspiration (ilham), whereas the Islamic gnostic admits a plurality of tools including sense perception, inspiration, reason, heart, and revelation. Second, regarding the method of attaining knowledge: the Sufi relies on physical exercises, dances, chants, and hardships, while the Islamic gnostic emphasizes full compliance with the outward and inward rules of Sharia. Third, regarding the ultimate goal: the Sufi claims to reach union (ittihad) with the divine essence, while the Islamic gnostic maintains that he remains a recipient of divine emanation (fayd) and honor, analogous to a master honoring a servant. The passage concludes with a summary: as a person ascends in purification, worship, and knowledge, God grants him supernatural knowledge that penetrates the unseen, and may even subject jinn or angels to his will. The author does not present a logical argument but rather an exposition of contrasting views.

Key Terms
الاتحاد بالذات الإلهية
الفيض الإلهي
تكريم السيد للعبد

Checkpoint

A) He becomes united with God's essence.
B) He receives knowledge from divine emanation and honor, like a master honoring a servant.
C) He attains a status equal to God.
D) He loses his individual identity.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic gnostic preserves the distinction between Creator and creature, rejecting any notion of mystical union.

Conclusion: The Path to Supernatural Knowledge
Conclusion: the reward of spiritual ascent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Islamic gnostic's view of the culmination of spiritual ascent as God-gifted supernatural knowledge and control over jinn/angels.

Core Idea
Supernatural knowledge is a gift from God, not a result of human effort alone.

The passage concludes with a summary of the Islamic gnostic's perspective: through purification and worship, God grants knowledge beyond the senses and even authority over unseen beings. This lesson examines that conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prerequisites
Find what the passage says about the conditions for receiving supernatural knowledge: ascending the ladder of purifications, acts of worship, and knowledge.
2
Identify the gift
Note what God grants: a supernatural knowledge that penetrates hidden veils, seeing unseen things from the world of malakut, and subjugation of jinn/angels.
3
Understand the source
Emphasize that this knowledge is a divine gift, not something earned automatically. The passage says 'God honors him'.
4
Compare with Sufi view
Recall the Sufi claim of union; here the gnostic remains a recipient of honor, not becoming divine. How does this conclusion reinforce the previous differences?

First Category of Divine Knowledge: The Constant Universal Mechanism
Author begins a two-part classification of divine knowledge emanation in the Quran, listing components of the first category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the components of the first category of divine knowledge emanation and explain the concept of 'al-ālī al-thābit al-‘āmm'.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge flows to all humans through a constant universal mechanism, not only to prophets.

This passage presents a classification of how divine knowledge emanates in the Quran. The author, in an addendum, divides it into two parts and elaborates on the first: a universal mechanism shared by all humanity. Understanding this list deepens our appreciation of how Allah guides all people through innate dispositions and common experiences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall classification
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The author states that the emanation of knowledge in the Quran can be divided into two parts. However, only the first part is detailed here. Note that the second part is absent from the available text.
This is an incomplete lesson; the second category would likely be discussed elsewhere.
2
Define the first category: al-ālī al-thābit al-‘āmm
This phrase translates as 'the constant universal mechanism'. It refers to a set of inspirations and innate knowledges that are fixed and common to all humans. The author uses this term to gather various sources of knowledge that are not exclusive to prophets or saints.
3
List the components mentioned
The author enumerates several components: fitra (innate disposition), ilhām (inspiration), baṣā'ir (insights), mīzān (balance), khayr wa-sharr (good and evil), fujūr wa-taqwā (wickedness and piety), ta‘līm al-lugha wa-al-bayān (teaching of language and expression), ru'yā manāmiyya i‘tiyādiyya (ordinary dream visions), hidāyat al-khalqa (guidance of creation), waḥy takwīnī (constitutive revelation), tarhīb wa-targhīb (intimidation and encouragement), and others. Count and note them.
The phrase 'and others' indicates the list is not exhaustive.
4
Reflect on the universality of this mechanism
Consider the implications: these components are given to all humans, not just believers or prophets. This shows that divine knowledge is accessible to everyone through creation, conscience, and common experience. The Quran itself points to these as signs for all people.
This classification is epistemological, explaining how humans gain knowledge of God and morality.

Commentary

Sufi Union vs. Islamic Gnostic Effusion
Author contrasts the Sufi claim of union with the Islamic gnostic's reliance on divine effusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Sufi claim of union (ittiḥād) from the Islamic gnostic's reliance on divine effusion (fayḍ) as a path to knowledge.

Core Idea
Union with God is rejected; the servant remains distinct, receiving knowledge as a gift.

This passage presents two competing epistemologies in Islamic mysticism. The Sufi claims that rigorous spiritual exercises lead to union with the Divine, while the Islamic gnostic insists that strict Shari‘a adherence keeps the servant-master relationship intact. Understanding this difference is essential for navigating debates about the nature of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two paths
Locate the contrasting descriptions of the Sufi and the Islamic gnostic. Note the practices each emphasizes: the Sufi uses exercises, dances, chants, and sufferings; the Islamic gnostic uses adherence to all acts of worship and Shari‘a rules, both outward and inward.
2
Analyze the goal of each path
The Sufi aims for ittiḥād (union with the Divine Essence). The Islamic gnostic aims for receiving knowledge from divine effusion (fayḍ) while maintaining the relationship of master to servant. This is the critical distinction.
The term ittiḥād is rejected by the Islamic gnostic because it implies pantheism.
3
Examine the passage's conclusion
The khulāṣa describes the reward for the Islamic gnostic: an unnatural knowledge that pierces the veils of the unseen, and possibly subjugating jinn or angels. Notice that the servant-master dynamic persists even at the highest levels.
This conclusion reinforces the rejection of union.
4
Synthesize the epistemological difference
Both paths claim to produce knowledge and unveiling, but the relationship to God differs. The Sufi's claim of union risks blurring the Creator-creature distinction, while the Islamic gnostic preserves it. This has implications for theological orthodoxy.
In Kalam, this aligns with the Ash'ari position that God remains transcendent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ ) الصوفي يقول ان التمارين والرقصات والاناشيد والمكابدات تؤدي الى المعرفة والكشف في حين يقول العارف الاسلامي ان الالتزام بالعبادات كلها وقواعد الشريعة ظاهرها وباطنها يؤدي الى المعرفة والكشف. ۳) ان الصوفي يقول انه يصل الى مرحلة الاتحاد بالذات الالهية . في حين يقول العارف الاسلامي انه في جميع الاحوال والمعارف والدرجات يبقى يتلقى المعرفة من الفيض الالهي والتكريم الالهي تكريم السيد للعبد . الخلاصة : ان الانسان إذا ارتقى في سلم الطهارات والعبادات والعلم يكرمه الله بمعرفة غير طبيعية تخترق الحجب الغائبة عن حواسنا فيرى اشياء غيبية من عالم الملكوت وقد يكرمه الله تعالى بان يسخر له بعض الجن او بعض الملائكة يطيعونه فيما يريد.
2) The Sufi says that exercises, dances, chants, and sufferings lead to knowledge and unveiling, whereas the Islamic gnostic says that adherence to all acts of worship and the rules of the Sharia, both outward and inward, leads to knowledge and unveiling. 3) The Sufi says that he reaches the stage of union with the Divine Essence, whereas the Islamic gnostic says that in all states, knowledge, and degrees, he remains receiving knowledge from divine effusion and divine honor, the honor of the master to the servant. Conclusion: When a person ascends the ladder of purifications, acts of worship, and knowledge, God honors him with an unnatural knowledge that penetrates the veils absent from our senses, so he sees unseen things from the world of the dominion, and God may honor him by subjugating some jinn or some angels who obey him in what he wills.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents two contrasting views on the path to divine knowledge. The Sufi view emphasizes rigorous physical and spiritual exercises, culminating in the claim of union (ittiḥād) with the Divine Essence. The Islamic gnostic (al-‘ārif al-islāmī) instead insists that strict adherence to the outward and inward rules of the Shari‘a is the means to knowledge, and that the knower never loses the servant-master relationship with God. The conclusion (khulāṣa) synthesizes these points: through purification, worship, and learning, God grants a supernatural knowledge that lifts the veils of the unseen, and may even subject jinn or angels to the seeker’s will. This maintains the distinction between Creator and creature, rejecting pantheistic union. In Islamic theology, this aligns with the orthodox position that God remains transcendent.

Key Takeaway

Two Types of Divine Knowledge Effusion in the Qur'an
The author divides divine knowledge inspiration into a fixed general type and a dynamic individual type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the fixed general mechanism and the dynamic living type of divine knowledge effusion, and identify examples of each from the passage.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge flows through universal and personal channels.

This addendum (istidrāk) provides a crucial classification of how God effuses knowledge. It divides the process into two distinct categories: a fixed general mechanism available to all humans and a dynamic living type that responds to individual circumstances. Understanding this distinction deepens our grasp of Islamic epistemology, bridging innate human cognition and prophetic revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and note the two main divisions: 'the fixed general mechanism' (al-ālī al-thābit al-‘āmm) and 'the dynamic living' (al-mutaḥarrik al-ḥayy). Observe how the author contrasts them.
2
List examples of the fixed general mechanism
Extract the examples under the first category: fiṭra, ilhām, baṣā’ir, mīzān, good/evil, etc. Ask: What do they share? They are universal, not limited to prophets.
These include innate disposition and ordinary dream visions.
3
List examples of the dynamic living type
Extract the examples under the second category: revelation through an angel for prophets, election, special education, private dream visions, etc. Note that it includes misguidance and guidance.
These depend on individual circumstances and divine choice.
4
Compare and reflect on implications
Compare the two lists. Notice that the second category includes misguidance as part of divine effusion. Reflect on the theological point: God is the source of all knowledge, but the mode differs. How does this affect our understanding of human responsibility?
This classification bridges general human cognition and special prophetic experiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إستدراك : يمكن تقسيم فيض المعرفة في القرآن الكريم على قسمين: الاول : الآلي الثابت العام : وهو المتعلق بمجموع الالهامات الثابتة لكل البشر ومنها ، الفطرة ، الالهام ، البصائر ، الميزان ، الخير والشر ، الفجور والتقوى ، تعليم اللغة والبيان ، الرؤيا المنامية الاعتيادية، هداية الخلقة ، الوحي التكويني ، الترهيب والترغيب وغيرها الثاني : المتحرك الحي : وهو المتعلق بالذات البشرية ككائن حي له ظروفه الخاصة كفرد ويدخل فيها : الوحي بملك للانبياء ، الاصطفاء ، التعليم الخاص المسدد ، الرؤيا المنامية الخاصة . العرفان ، الضلال والهدى
Addendum: The effusion of knowledge in the Noble Qur'an can be divided into two parts: First: The fixed general mechanism: It is related to the set of constant inspirations for all human beings, including: innate disposition (fitra), inspiration (ilhām), insights (baṣā'ir), the balance (mīzān), good and evil, immorality and piety, teaching of language and expression, ordinary dream visions, guidance of creation, formative revelation (waḥy takwīnī), intimidation and encouragement, and others. Second: The dynamic living: It is related to the human self as a living being having its own particular circumstances as an individual, and it includes: revelation through an angel for prophets, election, special guided education, special dream visions, gnosis, misguidance and guidance.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This addendum (istidrāk) provides a classification of divine knowledge effusion (fayḍ al-ma‘rifa) as presented in the Qur'an. The division is twofold. The first type is called 'the fixed general mechanism' (al-ālī al-thābit al-‘āmm), which is a set of constant inspirations granted to all humans. These include innate disposition (fiṭra), inspiration (ilhām), insights (baṣā’ir), the innate moral sense (mīzān), and even the teaching of language. The second type is 'the dynamic living' (al-mutaḥarrik al-ḥayy), which pertains to individuals in their specific circumstances. This includes prophetic revelation through an angel, divine election (iṣṭifā’), special education, and private dream visions. It also covers states like misguidance and guidance, indicating that even deviation is part of divine effusion. This classification bridges universal human cognition with special prophetic experiences.

Key Terms
الوحي التكويني
الفطرة
البصائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is part of the 'dynamic living' type of divine effusion?
A) Innate disposition (fiṭra)
B) Ordinary dream visions
C) Revelation through an angel for prophets
D) The balance (mīzān)

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic view of knowledge effusion includes both a universal fixed inspiration for all humans and a special dynamic inspiration for individuals, especially prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divine and Inspirational Sources of Knowledge
Enumeration of divine and inspirational knowledge sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the seven divine or inspirational sources of knowledge listed in the passage (Fitra, Wahy, Hidaya, Ta'lim, Ru'ya, Kutub Samawiyya/Anbiya, and Taraqqi).

Core Idea
Knowledge flows from divine grace through fitra, revelation, and spiritual elevation.

In this lesson, we focus on the first group of sources in the author's list: those that originate from God's direct action or guidance. These form the foundation of an Islamic epistemology that recognizes both innate and revealed knowledge. Understanding these sources helps us appreciate how Muslims have traditionally viewed the origins of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Locate the divine sources
In the Arabic passage, find items 5 (Fitra), 6 (Wahy), 7 (Hidaya), 8 (Ta'lim), 9 (Ru'ya), 18 (Kutub Samawiyya & Anbiya), and 19 (Taraqqi). Write down their Arabic names.
These are distinct but interconnected.
2
Define each source
For each item, extract the key description. For example, Fitra is 'a gentleness from God like a compass'. Wahy is 'divine internal signals'. Summarize each in your own words.
3
Categorize by type
Group them into subcategories: innate (Fitra), revealed (Wahy), guided (Hidaya), taught (Ta'lim), dream (Ru'ya), scriptural (Kutub/Anbiya), and spiritual elevation (Taraqqi). Notice how each contributes differently.
Some sources overlap; the author sees them as complementary.
4
Explain integration
Reflect on how these seven sources work together to form a complete divine epistemology. How does Fitra prepare humans for revelation? How do dreams confirm prophetic truth?
This integration is key to the author's holistic view.

ه ( الفطرة : لطف من الله سبحانه وهي كالبوصلة النفسية والجسدية الثابتة في صميم الخلقة مودعة في كل فرد من أفراد العالم تجعل الانسان يتحرك وينمو ويتطور داخل اطار ثابت .٦ ) الوحي : وهو عبارة عن إشارات داخلية ربانية تساهم في تكامل المعرفة البشرية ويشترك الوحي بجميع انواعه العام والخاص في المساهمة في المعرفة البشرية. وهي .) الهداية الالهية في المعرفة دلالة لطيفة ومصدر من مصادر الادراك أشبه بالنور الذي يقود الانسان الى ما قرره الله سبحانه.۸ ( التعليم : أي التسديد الالهي للانسان ولاسيما العلماء في جميع اتجاهاتهم والمتمثل بالومضات الروحية للحظات من الزمن تمكن الانسان من حل الاشكالات المعرفية الصعبة والمنعطفات العصية على الحل. (۹) الرؤيا المنامية عدها القرآن أحد مصادر المعرفة فهي تسهم في تكامل أدلة الهداية وبراهين اضافية عن تدخل الغيب الالهي في المعرفة .۱۰ ) القلب أحد عظماء المستشارين للنفس وهو مختص بالامور العاطفية وله قوانينه الخاصة .۱۱ ) الصدر يعد مكمن ومخزن لكثير من المعلومات والضغائن والوساوس والافكار.۱۲ ) الغريزة الجنسية لها تأثير واضح وأصيل في الكثير من القرارات التي تتخذها النفس. ۱۳ ) الشياطين لهم دور تخريبي في نظام المعرفة وفي القرارات الخاطئة التي تتخذها النفس ويتجلى في قرارات النفس السيئة او الامارة بالسوء .١٤) التفكر والنظر العقلي واستخدام التجارب : ان هذا اللون من العلم الذي يسمى المعرفة الكسبية يحصل عليها الانسان بالتدريج من خلال الانسان وتجاربه العلمية والحياتية تمثل فيضا متجدد من العلم والمعارف تسهم في تكامل الصور المعرفية. ١٥ ) النفس شخصية مستقلة وهي حصن المعرفة الاصيل ووعاؤها الكبير ومحركها الاول هي السيد الاكبر في أعماق الذات . لها سلطان على المادة البدن وهي نافذة على الغيب وما وراء الطبيعة فالمعارف تأتي الى النفس من كل حدب وصوب والقوى تتجاذبها ليلا ونهارا.١٦ ) الميزان يكشف الاخطاء سواء منها خطأ الاحساس بالواقع أو خطأ المعطيات المقدمة الى النفس أو خطأ أي من الأدوات . إن الوهم هو العدول عن الطريق عمدا أو سهوا . أما الخطأ فهو ما ليس للانسان فيه قصد واحد اسبابه سرعة عمل الميزان أو خطأ في المعطيات التي استلمتها الحواس وقدمتها الى الميزان مما يؤدي الى خطأ في القرار الذي اتخذته النفس.۱۷ ) الذاكرة هيئة للنفس بها يمكن للانسان ان يحفظ ما يقتنيه من معرفة ويثبت ما يؤدي الى الفهم وهي كالحفظ وتعد واحدة من أهم عناصر النفس. إنها حافظة ملحقة بالنفس لها أسرار وقوانين تجعل الانسان يتذكر ما يصلح امور حياته ويتناسى ما يفسدها .١٨) الكتب السماوية والانبياء لها دور مهم في تكامل المعرفة من حيث ارشاد النفس بعدد هائل من الحقائق المطلقة. ۱۹ ) ان الانسان إذا ارتقى في سلم الطهارات والعبادات والعلم يكرمه الله بمعرفة غير طبيعية تخترق الحجب الغائبة عن حواسنا فيرى اشياء غيبية من
5) Fitra: a gentleness from God, like a psychological and physical compass fixed in the core of creation, deposited in every individual of the world, making man move, grow, and develop within a fixed framework. 6) Revelation (Wahy): divine internal signals that contribute to the integration of human knowledge; revelation in all its types, general and specific, participates in human knowledge. 7) Divine guidance in knowledge: a subtle indication and a source of perception akin to light that leads man to what God has decreed. 8) Teaching (Ta'lim): divine support for man, especially scholars, in all their directions, manifested as spiritual flashes in moments of time that enable man to solve difficult cognitive problems and intractable turns. 9) Dream visions (Ru'ya Manamiyya): the Quran counts them as a source of knowledge; they contribute to the integration of guidance proofs and additional evidence of divine intervention in knowledge. 10) The heart (Qalb): one of the great counselors of the soul, specialized in emotional matters, with its own laws. 11) The chest (Sadr): a repository and storehouse for much information, grudges, whispers, and thoughts. 12) Sexual instinct: has a clear and original influence on many decisions taken by the soul. 13) Devils: have a destructive role in the system of knowledge and in the wrong decisions taken by the soul, manifested in the bad or commanding-to-evil decisions of the soul. 14) Reflection, intellectual consideration, and use of experiences: this type of knowledge called acquired knowledge (ma'rifa kasbiyya) is obtained by man gradually through his efforts and his scientific and life experiences, representing a renewed flood of science and knowledge that contribute to the integration of cognitive forms. 15) The soul (Nafs): an independent personality, the original fortress of knowledge, its great container, and its primary mover; it is the great master in the depths of the self. It has authority over the material body, and it is a window to the unseen and the metaphysical; knowledge comes to the soul from every direction, and forces pull at it night and day. 16) The Scale (Mizan): reveals errors, whether from sensory error in reality, error in data presented to the soul, or error in any of the tools. Illusion (Wahm) is turning away from the path intentionally or unintentionally. As for error (Khata'), it is what man has no intention in; its causes are the speed of the scale's functioning or error in the data received by the senses and presented to the scale, leading to error in the decision taken by the soul. 17) Memory (Dhakhira): a state of the soul by which man can preserve what he acquires of knowledge and establish what leads to understanding; it is like retention and is considered one of the most important elements of the soul. It is a repository attached to the soul, with secrets and laws that make man remember what is beneficial for his life and forget what spoils it. 18) The heavenly books and prophets: have an important role in the integration of knowledge in terms of guiding the soul with a huge number of absolute truths. 19) When a man ascends the ladder of purifications, devotions, and knowledge, God honors him with non-natural knowledge that penetrates the veils absent from our senses, so he sees unseen things.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of sources of knowledge, presented as a list of 15 items (numbers 5 through 19, continuing from a previous page). The author, likely an Islamic philosopher, categorizes both divine and human elements that contribute to human cognition. The list begins with 'Fitra' (innate disposition), described as a divine subtlety and a compass embedded in human nature, guiding development within a fixed framework. Next is 'Wahy' (revelation), divine internal signals aiding knowledge integration. Then 'Hidaya' (divine guidance), a subtle source resembling light. 'Ta'lim' (teaching) refers to divine support for scholars via spiritual flashes. 'Ru'ya Manamiyya' (dream visions) are recognized by the Quran as knowledge sources. The heart (Qalb) is an emotional counselor, while the chest (Sadr) stores information and sentiments. Sexual instinct influences decisions, and devils (Shayatin) can corrupt knowledge. 'Tafakkur' (reflection) and experience yield acquired knowledge. The soul (Nafs) is the independent container and master of the self, with authority over the body and access to the unseen. The 'Mizan' (scale) detects errors from senses or data processing; illusion is intentional digression, error is unintentional. 'Dhakhira' (memory) preserves knowledge. Heavenly books and prophets guide with absolute truths. Finally, ascending in purity and worship grants non-natural knowledge of the unseen. Each source is briefly defined but collectively presents an integrated Islamic epistemology where both spiritual and empirical elements are acknowledged.

الميزان

Human Faculties and External Influences on Knowledge
Enumeration of human faculties and external influences on knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and describe eight human faculties and external influences that shape knowledge according to the passage (Qalb, Sadr, Ghariza, Shayateen, Tafakkur, Nafs, Mizan, Dhakhira).

Core Idea
The soul's faculties and external forces like instinct and devils both aid and hinder knowledge acquisition.

The second half of the author's list focuses on internal faculties of the soul (heart, chest, soul itself, memory, and the scale) and external influences (sexual instinct, devils, and acquired knowledge through reflection). Understanding these helps us see how knowledge is processed, stored, and sometimes corrupted.

Guided Path
1
Locate the human faculties and influences
In the passage, find items 10 (Qalb), 11 (Sadr), 12 (Ghariza), 13 (Shayateen), 14 (Tafakkur), 15 (Nafs), 16 (Mizan), 17 (Dhakhira). Write their Arabic names.
These items follow the divine sources.
2
Distinguish internal vs external
Categorize each as either an internal faculty of the soul (Qalb, Sadr, Nafs, Mizan, Dhakhira) or an external influence (Ghariza, Shayateen, Tafakkur/experience). Notice that Tafakkur is a human action, but it is listed as a source of acquired knowledge.
The author sees both internal and external factors as shaping knowledge.
3
Explain the function of each
For each item, write a one-sentence function. For example, the heart (Qalb) advises on emotional matters; the chest (Sadr) stores grudges and ideas; the sexual instinct influences decisions; devils corrupt; reflection yields acquired knowledge; the soul (Nafs) is the central container; the scale (Mizan) detects errors; memory (Dhakhira) preserves knowledge.
4
Analyze the mizan
Focus on item 16. How does the scale differentiate between illusion (wahm) and error (khata')? What causes error according to the author? Reflect on how this faculty is essential for evaluating knowledge.
The mizan is a cognitive faculty that checks sensory data and reasoning.

Imam Ali's Conditional Refutation of Jabr
Author cites Imam Ali's conditional refutation of jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Imam Ali's conditional argument against jabr and identify its logical form as a reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
If jabr were true, moral responsibility collapses — therefore jabr is false.

This passage from a modern philosophical work raises the ancient question of free will vs. predestination. The author quotes Imam Ali's sharp refutation of jabr using a conditional argument: if human actions were compelled, then the entire structure of reward, punishment, command, and blame would be meaningless. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it functions as a reduction to absurdity (burhān al-khalf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis being attacked
Read the Arabic and find the description of jabr. The author phrases the question: 'هل الإنسان مجبر أم مفوض?' (Is man compelled or delegated?). The target is the doctrine that humans are compelled in all actions.
2
Extract the conditional statement
Imam Ali says: 'لو كان كذلك لبطل الثواب والعقاب' (If it were so, then reward and punishment would be invalid). This is a conditional (sharṭiyya) with antecedent (jabr) and consequent (invalidity of moral order). List the other consequents: command, prohibition, blame, praise, etc.
The antecedent is the assumption of jabr; the consequent is a set of impossible outcomes.
3
Recognize the absurd consequence
The argument leads to absurdities: no blame for wrongdoer, no praise for good doer, and even reversal: 'ولكان المحسن أولى باللائمة من المذنب والمذنب أولى بالاحسان من المحسن' (the good person would be more deserving of blame than the sinner). This is clearly false in Islam.
4
Understand the overall argument as reductio ad absurdum
Imam Ali uses burhān al-khalf: assume jabr, derive contradictions with clear Islamic principles (reward/punishment), therefore jabr is false. The logical form is modus tollens: if P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
This is a valid conditional inference.
5
Connect to the second quote
The second quote categorizes deeds and states that obligatory acts are by Allah's decree, but sins and virtues are not purely compelled. This provides a nuanced position avoiding both jabr and tafwīḍ, preserving moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال قديم وجواب جديد
يطرح في هذا الموضع السؤال التقليدي المعروف وهو هل الانسان مخير في قراراته ام مسير؟ وبعبارة الفقهاء القدماء هل هو مجبرام مفوض؟ وأرى من المناسب قبل ان نعطي جوابا قاطعا حول هذا الموضوع ان نستعرض بعض النصوص . قال الامام علي بن ابي طلاب السلام في بيان بطلان الجبر (لو كان كذلك لبطل الثواب والعقاب والامر والنهي والزجر ولسقط معنى الوعد والوعيد ولم تكن على مسيء لائمة ولا لمحسن محمدة ولكان المحسن أولى باللائمة من المذنب والمذنب أولى بالاحسان من المحسن تلك مقالة عبدة الاوثان وخصماء الرحمن وقال السّلام ) الاعمال) ثلاثة فرائض وفضائل ومعاص فاما الفرائض فبأمر الله ومشيئته وبرضاه وبعلمه وقدره يعملها العبد
۱ بحار الانوار، محمد باقر المجلسي، مصدر سابق ج ۵ ص ۱۳
An ancient question and a new answer. At this point, the well-known traditional question is posed: Is man free in his decisions or compelled? In the expression of the ancient jurists: Is he compelled or delegated? I see it appropriate before giving a definitive answer on this topic to review some texts. Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib, peace be upon him, said in explaining the invalidity of compulsion (jabr): 'If it were so, then reward and punishment, command and prohibition, and deterrence would be invalid, and the meaning of promise and threat would fall away, and there would be no blame for the wrongdoer nor praise for the doer of good. And the doer of good would be more deserving of blame than the sinner, and the sinner more deserving of good than the doer of good. That is the saying of the idol worshippers and the enemies of the Merciful.' And he (peace be upon him) said: 'Deeds are three: obligatory acts, supererogatory acts, and sins. As for the obligatory acts, they are by the command of Allah, His will, His pleasure, His knowledge, and His decree; the servant performs them.'
1 Bihar al-Anwar, Muhammad Baqir al-Majlisi, previous source, vol. 5, p. 13.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical presentation: the author raises the classical theological question of free will versus determinism (jabr vs. tafwīḍ) and then introduces textual evidence from Imam Ali. The author notes that the question is traditionally phrased by jurists as 'Is man compelled or delegated?' Before giving a final answer, he reviews texts. The first quote from Imam Ali is a conditional argument: if human actions were compelled (jabr), then the entire system of reward, punishment, command, prohibition, blame, and praise would be invalid, and moral distinctions would collapse. This would lead to absurd consequences, such as the good person being more blameworthy than the sinner. Imam Ali labels such a view as the saying of idol worshippers. The second quote categorizes deeds into three types and states that obligatory acts are by Allah's command, will, pleasure, knowledge, and decree, implying that while the servant performs them, they are within divine sovereignty. This provides a nuanced position that avoids both pure jabr and pure tafwīḍ. The passage ends with a footnote reference to Bihar al-Anwar.

Key Terms
الجبر
التفويض
الثواب والعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Ali's argument, what would be a consequence of the belief in absolute compulsion (jabr)?
A) Humans would be praised for their good deeds.
B) The sinner would deserve punishment.
C) The doer of good would be more worthy of blame than the sinner.
D) Obligatory acts would be purely by human choice.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ali's conditional refutation shows that belief in absolute compulsion (jabr) is incompatible with the Islamic principles of reward and punishment, thus proving that humans must have some degree of free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Imam Ali's conditional form, construct a conditional argument against the extreme Qadariyya position (tafwīḍ) that humans act completely independently of divine will. What absurd consequences would follow?
?
In a debate about free will, a student claims: 'If Allah has knowledge of all future events, then humans cannot have free will.' Identify the antecedent and consequent, and evaluate whether this conditional argument is valid. Why or why not?

The Middle Path Between Compulsion and Delegation
Author explains the intermediate position between jabr and tafwid using an analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the middle position between jabr and tafwid using the servant analogy and identify its components.

Core Idea
The middle path is a divine grace balancing freedom within boundaries.

This passage presents a classic dialectical exchange with Imam al-Sadiq, rejecting both jabr and tafwid. The author clarifies the middle position using a vivid analogy of a servant sent to the market. This lesson will help you deconstruct that analogy and understand how it models human free will under divine command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rejected positions
Locate the questioner's two propositions (jabr and tafwid) and the Imam's rejection of both. Note that both extremes are repudiated.
2
Note the 'lutf' alternative
The Imam offers a third answer: 'A grace from your Lord between that.' This is the key term for the middle position.
Lutf in kalam often means divine grace that facilitates obedience without compulsion.
3
Deconstruct the servant analogy
Map each element: master = God, servant = human, money = life, market = worldly life, accounting = judgment. The master knows but does not intervene.
4
Define redefined jabr and tafwid
The author redefines jabr as commands/prohibitions and tafwid as freedom within those limits. Note the shift from classical meanings.
In classical kalam, jabr means compulsion; here it is softened to divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سأل رجل الامام الصادق - ع - أجبر الله العباد على المعاصي ؟ قال لا . فقال : فوض اليهم أمرهم ؟ قال لا . قال فماذا ؟ قال : لطف من ربك بين ذلك ومن اجل توضيح الجواب سنعطي المثال الآتي عندك خادم تريد ان تختبر صدقه في الخدمة واخلاصه في العمل من كذبه وخيانته فتعطيه مبلغا من المال ليقضي بها الحوائج في السوق فيتصرف بكل حرية من دون رقيب عليه وانت تعلم هل هو خان الامانة أو صانها حين يأتي وقت الحساب . فالتخويل الالهي بشرطه وهكذا هي مسألة الجبر والتفويض: ان الله سبحانه وتعالى خول الانسان ان يتصرف في الحياة الدنيا بعد ان رسم له معالم ما يريد الله ويحب وما يكره الله وما يرفض، وبعبارة أوضح : جبر يتمثل بمجموع الأوامر والنواهي والترهيب والترغيب وتفويض يتمثل بحرية التسوق في الحياة الدنيا وَأَنْ لَيْسَ لِلْإِنْسَانِ إِلَّا مَا سَعَى ، وَأَنْ سَعْيَهُ سَوْفَ يُرَى
A man asked Imam al-Sadiq, peace be upon him: 'Did God compel the servants to sins?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'Did He delegate the matter to them?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'Then what?' He said: 'A grace from your Lord between that.' And to clarify the answer, we will give the following example: You have a servant whom you want to test his honesty in service and his sincerity in work from his lying and betrayal. So you give him an amount of money to spend on needs in the market, and he acts with complete freedom without an overseer, while you know whether he betrayed the trust or preserved it when the time of accounting comes. So the divine authorization with its condition. And such is the issue of compulsion and delegation: That God, glorified and exalted, authorized man to act in this worldly life after laying out for him the landmarks of what God wills and loves and what God dislikes and rejects. In clearer terms: Compulsion is represented by the sum of commands and prohibitions, intimidation and encouragement, and delegation is represented by the freedom of shopping in this worldly life. And that man has only what he strives for, and that his striving will be seen.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where a questioner seeks clarification on the Islamic doctrine of divine decree and human free will. The author begins by recounting a conversation with Imam al-Sadiq, who rejects both absolute compulsion (jabr) and absolute delegation (tafwid). Instead, the Imam describes a middle position as a 'grace from your Lord between that.' To explain, the author presents an analogy: a master gives a servant money and sends him to the market without supervision, not because the master is unaware of the servant's actions, but to test his trustworthiness. Similarly, God has given humans the capacity to act freely within the framework of His commands and prohibitions. The passage then defines jabr as the totality of divine commands, prohibitions, warnings, and encouragements that set boundaries, while tafwid is the freedom to choose actions within those boundaries. The conclusion cites Quranic verses to emphasize that each person's effort is accounted for. This teaching guards against two extremes: that God compels every action, or that humans are independent of divine will.

Key Terms
جبر
تفويض
لطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the master's knowledge of the servant's actions symbolize in the jabr-tafwid issue?
A) That God compels every action of the servant.
B) That God delegates all authority to the servant.
C) That God knows the outcome but allows free choice within boundaries.
D) That God is unaware of the servant's actions.

Key Takeaway
The middle path teaches that human freedom is real but bounded by divine commands, and God's foreknowledge does not compel.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a new analogy using the same structure (master, servant, test, accountability) to explain the concept of qadr (divine decree) to someone who thinks it means either total compulsion or total freedom.
?
Identify in the passage the two elements that correspond to the redefined jabr and tafwid in the analogy, and explain how the 'illa of the master's test relates to God's purpose in creating humans with free will.

Parsing Quranic Epistemology Sources
Lists epistemological sources in a Quranic diagram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify major sources of knowledge in Quranic epistemology as presented in the diagram.

Core Idea
Quranic epistemology integrates reason, revelation, and experience as complementary sources.

This lesson introduces a diagram from a modern work comparing Quranic and philosophical epistemology. The diagram lists key sources of knowledge mentioned in the Quran, such as al-ʿaql (intellect), al-waḥy (revelation), and al-baṣāʾir (insights). Your task is to parse the diagram's components and understand how they fit into an Islamic theory of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Extract all listed sources
From the Arabic text, identify each term that represents a source of knowledge. Look for terms like العقل, الوحي, البصائر, الإلهام, التجارب البشرية, الأنبياء, الكتب السماوية, الرؤيا المنام, الميزان, الذاكرة.
Some terms like 'القطرة' (droplet) and 'الصدر' (chest) may be diagram labels or artifacts; focus on the clear epistemological terms.
2
Categorize by type
Group the sources into categories: rational (العقل), revealed (الوحي, الأنبياء, الكتب السماوية), experiential (التجارب البشرية, الرؤيا المنام, الإلهام), and spiritual (البصائر, الميزان, الذاكرة).
The diagram may not specify categories; you need to infer based on Islamic epistemology.
3
Analyze relationships
Consider how the sources relate to each other. The diagram title 'نظرية المعرفة بين القرآن والفلسفة' suggests a comparison. Note any arrows or connections (if present in original). The term '1 +' might indicate a grouping.
The OCR is fragmentary; relationships may be missing.
4
Assess completeness
Compare this list with classical Islamic epistemology. For instance, al-Ghazali in al-Munqidh min al-Dalal mentions senses, reason, and revelation. Does this diagram include all? Are there sources like consensus (ijma) or qiyas?
This helps evaluate the diagram's scope.

Agreement on the Foundations of Knowledge
Author enumerates first three points of agreement between Quran and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Quranic and philosophical views on the importance, central question, and means of knowledge as presented in the six points.

Core Idea
The Quran and philosophy agree on epistemology's centrality, the question of perception, and the dual means of sense and reason.

This lesson focuses on the first three points of agreement between the Quran and philosophical theories regarding epistemology. These points establish a common ground on the fundamental role of knowledge theory, the core question it addresses, and the primary instruments humans use to acquire knowledge. Understanding these points helps you see where revelation and rational inquiry converge in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three domains of agreement
Points 1-3 cover: (1) the importance of epistemology as a pillar, (2) the central question of how the soul perceives realities, and (3) the two basic means: sense and reason. Notice the author's numbered structure.
The Quranic evidence is spread across verses, here summarized by the author.
2
Extract the Quranic proof for each point
For point 1, the author cites 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' (51:21). For point 2, no verse is given but the question is stated. For point 3, the author describes the vital role of sense and reason, noting that loss of a sense loses that knowledge.
The third point clarifies that sense is foundational, except for the theory of dormant instincts.
3
Compare the philosophical and Quranic emphasis
Both traditions treat epistemology as foundational. The Quran distributes its theory across verses, while philosophy makes it an explicit discipline. The agreement shows that knowledge acquisition is not contradictory to revelation.
This is a parsing exercise: identify the common ground without assuming total identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نظرية المعرفة بين القرآن والفلسفة (۱) نقاط الاتفاق بين القرآن ونظريات الفلاسفة (۱) اتفقت نظريات الفلاسفة مجتمعة والقرآن الكريم في أهمية نظرية المعرفة، فعلى صعيد الفلسفة كانت نظرية المعرفة أحد أعمدتها الأساسية، وكذلك القرآن الكريم الذي بث نظريته في المعرفة مفرقة في ثنيات آياته هنا وهناك وأعطى إشارة واضحة لرسم صورة شاملة لمعالم نظريته في هذا المضمار فقال (وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون). (۲) اتفقت نظريات الفلاسفة جميعها مع نظرية القرآن على أن محور نظرية المعرفة هو الجواب على سؤال هذه الدراسة وهو السؤال المركزي: كيف تدرك النفس حقائق الأشياء؟ (۳) اتفقت جميع نظريات الفلاسفة مع نظرية القرآن الكريم على أن الوسيلتين الأساسيتين للمعرفة عند الإنسان هما الحس والعقل، وأكدا على دور حيوي للحس وقالا إن الإنسان إذا فقد حاسة فقد المعرفة الخاصة بهذه الحاسة، وأن الحس هو قاعدة التصورات البشرية فيما شذت عن هذه القاعدة نظرية الغرائز الضامرة. (٤) اتفق الفلاسفة جميعا مع القرآن الكريم على أن الإنسان يولد خالي الذهن عن كل معرفة (والله أخرجكم من بطون أمهاتكم لا تعلمون شيئا) (٥) اتفق الذكر الحكيم مع نظرية الاستذكار الأفلاطونية على إمكان تذكر المعلومات من عالم إلى آخر. (٦) اتفق القرآن الكريم مع نظرية الأفكار الفطرية على ذكر لفظ الفطرة.
Epistemology between the Quran and Philosophy (1) Points of Agreement between the Quran and Philosophical Theories: (1) The philosophical theories collectively and the Holy Quran agreed on the importance of epistemology. At the philosophical level, epistemology was one of its fundamental pillars, and likewise the Holy Quran disseminated its theory of knowledge scattered in the folds of its verses here and there and gave a clear indication to draw a comprehensive picture of its theory in this domain, saying (And in yourselves, do you not see?). (2) All philosophical theories agreed with the Quranic theory that the focus of epistemology is the answer to the question of this study, which is the central question: How does the soul perceive the realities of things? (3) All philosophical theories agreed with the Holy Quran's theory that the two basic means of knowledge for humans are sense perception and reason, and they emphasized the vital role of sense perception, saying that if a person loses a sense, he loses knowledge specific to that sense, and that sense perception is the foundation of human concepts, except that the theory of dormant instincts deviated from this rule. (4) All philosophers agreed with the Holy Quran that humans are born with an empty mind regarding all knowledge: (And Allah brought you out of your mothers' wombs while you knew nothing). (5) The Wise Remembrance agreed with the Platonic theory of recollection on the possibility of recalling information from one world to another. (6) The Holy Quran agreed with the theory of innate ideas on mentioning the word 'fitrah' (innate disposition).
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage enumerates six points of agreement between the Quran and philosophical theories regarding epistemology (نظرية المعرفة). The author follows a numbered list format (1-6). The first point states that both the Quran and philosophers consider epistemology (theory of knowledge) to be a fundamental pillar. The author notes that while philosophers have a distinct field, the Quran's theory is scattered throughout its verses, with a reference to the verse 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' (Quran 51:21). The second point identifies the central question of epistemology as 'How does the soul perceive the realities of things?' Both the Quran and philosophers focus on this question. The third point asserts agreement on the two basic means of knowledge: sense perception (al-hiss) and reason (al-aql). They stress the vital role of sense perception, noting that loss of a sense leads to loss of corresponding knowledge, and that sense perception is the foundation (qa'ida) of human concepts. The author mentions that the theory of 'dormant instincts' (الغرائز الضامرة) deviates from this view. The fourth point cites the Quranic verse (Quran 16:78) to argue that humans are born with an empty mind (khaali al-dhihn), agreeing with the tabula rasa concept. The fifth point finds agreement with Plato's theory of recollection (anamnesis), interpreted as recalling information from another world. The sixth point notes that the Quran uses the term 'fitrah' (innate disposition), aligning with the theory of innate ideas.

Key Terms
نظرية المعرفة
الحس والعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The only means is reason
B) The only means is sense
C) The two basic means are sense and reason
D) The two basic means are revelation and intuition

Key Takeaway
The Quran and philosophers agree that epistemology is fundamental, that its central question concerns how the soul perceives realities, and that sense and reason are the primary tools of knowledge.

Agreement on the Origin and Innateness of Knowledge
Author enumerates the last three points of agreement between Quran and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three remaining points of agreement (empty mind, recollection, fitrah) and evaluate their relationship to Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Humans begin with empty minds, yet the Quran affirms both recollection of past knowledge and innate fitrah.

The last three points of agreement (4-6) address the starting point of human knowledge, the possibility of recalling knowledge from an earlier existence, and the innate disposition. These points show how the Quran engages with concepts similar to tabula rasa, Platonic recollection, and innate ideas, while grounding them in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three points
Points 4, 5, and 6 handle: (4) empty mind at birth, (5) possibility of recalling knowledge from another world, (6) agreement on the term 'fitrah' as innate ideas.
The author presents them sequentially with Quranic support.
2
Examine the Quranic proof for point 4
The verse 'And Allah brought you out of your mothers' wombs while you knew nothing' (16:78) is cited to show that humans start with no knowledge.
This is a direct textual proof from the Quran.
3
Analyze point 5: recollection
The author says the Quran agrees with Plato's theory of recollection (anamnesis) on the possibility of recalling information from one world to another. No specific verse is given here, but it implies a pre-existent realm of knowledge.
This point is controversial in Islamic orthodoxy; not all scholars accept recollection.
4
Analyze point 6: fitrah and innate ideas
The Quran uses the word 'fitrah'. The author claims this aligns with the theory of innate ideas. Fitrah often means the primordial nature inclined to tawhid, but here it is used loosely.
Be aware of the shift: fitrah traditionally means innate disposition towards God, not a set of ideas.

(Quran 51:21). The second point identifies the central question of epistemology as 'How does the soul perceive the realities of things?' Both the Quran and philosophers focus on this question. The third point asserts agreement on the two basic means of knowledge: sense perception (al-hiss) and reason (al-aql). They stress the vital role of sense perception, noting that loss of a sense leads to loss of corresponding knowledge, and that sense perception is the foundation (qa'ida) of human concepts. The author mentions that the theory of 'dormant instincts' (الغرائز الضامرة) deviates from this view. The fourth point cites the Quranic verse (Quran 16:78) to argue that humans are born with an empty mind (khaali al-dhihn), agreeing with the tabula rasa concept. The fifth point finds agreement with Plato's theory of recollection (anamnesis), interpreted as recalling information from another world. The sixth point notes that the Quran uses the term 'fitrah' (innate disposition), aligning with the theory of innate ideas.

Key Terms
خالي الذهن
الفطرة

Key Takeaway
The Quran affirms that humans begin with no knowledge, but also allows for recollection from a prior world and uses the term fitrah, which the author aligns with innate ideas, though the classical interpretation differs.

Quran on Inner Psychological Factors and Knowledge
Lists agreements 7, 11, 12 on inner psychological factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain how the Quran agrees with philosophical theories on inner psychological factors influencing knowledge and decision-making.

Core Idea
The Quran affirms that hidden instincts and innate principles shape human cognition.

This lesson explores three points of agreement between the Quran and philosophical theories regarding internal factors in knowledge: the role of dormant instincts (including sexual instinct) and latent cognitive desires. The Quran not only acknowledges these inner dimensions but also grounds them in divine awareness, as seen in the verse 'And Allah knows what you reveal and what you conceal.' Understanding these agreements helps bridge Quranic epistemology with theories of human psychology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three agreements on internal factors
From the passage, locate points 7, 11, and 12. They all deal with inner psychological elements: hidden thinking (point 7), sexual instinct (point 11), and latent cognitive desires (point 12).
Point 10 also touches on innate principles, but it is covered in the next lesson.
2
Extract the Quranic basis for each agreement
For point 7, the Quranic proof is the verse 'Allah knows what you reveal and what you conceal.' For points 11 and 12, no specific verse is cited, but the agreement is stated directly. Consider why the author might use a verse for some points and not others.
3
Analyze the logical relationship
Each agreement is a claim that the Quran supports a particular philosophical theory. Evaluate the strength of the support: does the Quran explicitly endorse the theory, or does it merely share a common theme?
The author uses the word 'agreed' (اتفق) which suggests harmony, not dependence.
4
Compare philosophical terms with Quranic terms
Notice that the Quran uses its own language: for innate principles, it says 'basair' (insights) rather than the philosophers' term. This shows the Quran's unique conceptual framework while overlapping in meaning.

This passage enumerates six points of agreement between the Quran and various philosophical theories on knowledge. Each point is a complete comparison: 
7. The Quran acknowledges inner psychological factors (like instincts) influencing decisions, consistent with the dormant instinct theory, citing a verse about Allah's knowledge of hidden matters.

Quran on External and Divine Sources of Knowledge
Lists agreements 8, 9, 10 on external and divine sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain how the Quran agrees with philosophical theories on external and divine sources of knowledge, including objective reality and divine illumination.

Core Idea
The Quran values objective reality and divine illumination as sources of knowledge.

This lesson covers three agreements that emphasize external and transcendent inputs to human knowledge: the role of objective reality (point 9), divine illumination and unseen forces (point 8), and innate rational principles (point 10) which, while internal, are seen as divinely endowed. The Quranic term 'basair' (insights) is introduced as a key concept. These agreements show that the Quran does not limit knowledge to sensory experience but includes metaphysical dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three agreements on external and divine sources
From the passage, points 8, 9, and 10. Point 8: unseen forces (divine illumination). Point 9: objective reality. Point 10: innate rational principles (basair).
Point 10 also has an internal aspect, but it is listed here because it involves a source (basair) that is given by God.
2
Connect each to the corresponding philosophical theory
Point 8 aligns with 'ishraq' (illumination) and Sufi thought. Point 9 with dialectical materialism's emphasis on objective reality. Point 10 with innate ideas and latent power theories.
The author cleverly shows Quranic cognizance of diverse traditions.
3
Examine the Quranic term 'basair'
Point 10 says the Quran calls primary rational principles 'basair'. This term appears in surahs like al-An'am (6:104) and al-Isra (17:102). Reflect on how this term differs from philosophical terms like 'aql (intellect) or fitra.
4
Assess the significance of these agreements for epistemology
Consider how these agreements support a holistic Quranic epistemology that incorporates empirical, rational, and revealed sources. Each agreement fills a gap: objective reality grounds sense perception; divine illumination provides transcendent insight; innate principles give a priori foundations.

The Quranic Affirmation of Absolute Truth vs Sophism
First difference: Quran vs sophism on absolute truth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core difference between Quranic epistemology and sophism, and construct a Quranic refutation of sophistry.

Core Idea
The Quran affirms absolute truths; sophism denies them.

In this lesson, we contrast the Quran's assertion of objective, absolute truths about existence, creation, and knowledge with the sophist claim that no such truths exist. This is the first point of difference listed by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic claim
What does the Quran affirm about truth? It affirms absolute truths about the universe, life, creation, and knowledge.
The passage states 'the Quran affirmed the existence of absolute truths'.
2
Identify the sophist claim
What do sophists deny? They deny all absolute truths completely and in detail.
3
Find the Quranic response
Look at the verse cited (22:8) and see how it criticizes arguing without knowledge, guidance, or a book.
The verse serves as a template for refuting any epistemological relativism.
4
Apply to a contemporary context
Think of a modern relativist argument (e.g., 'truth is subjective') and formulate a Quranic response using the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲ نقاط الاختلاف بين القرآن ونظريات الفلاسفة
(۱) اختلفت نظرية القرآن الكريم عن مقولات السفسطة كلها فالقرآن قال بوجود حقائق مطلقة عن الكون والحياة والخلق والمعرفة في حين نفت السوفسطائية ذلك جملة وتفصيلا . ويمكن ان نستلهم ردا قرآنيا على السفسطة في الآية المباركة (وَمِنَ النَّاسِ مَنْ يُجَادِلُ فِي اللهِ بِغَيْرِ عِلْمٍ وَلَا هُدًى وَلَا كِتَابٍ 
۲ ) اختلف القرآن الكريم مع نظرية الاستذكار حول مفهوم التذكر. فقال القرآن ان الانسان في حياة ما بعد الموت يتذكر حياة ما قبل الموت . في حين قالت نظرية افلاطون ان الانسان يتذكر من عالم ما قبل الولادة واختلفت نظرية افلاطون عن الذكر الحكيم في ماهية المعلومات التي يتذكرها الانسان فقال القرآن ان الانسان يتذكر اعماله الصالحة او الطالحة التي عملها في الحياة . قالت نظرية افلاطون ان الانسان يتذكر المثل . واختلف مصطلح التذكر فقد قال افلاطون ان التذكر استرجاع في حين قصد القرآن الكريم الحفظ . قالت اغلب نظريات الفلسفة التي درسناها في هذا البحث : ان الوجود يساوي المادة وان المعرفة ظاهرة مادية تتحقق اثر التعاملات الفيزيائية والكيميائية والفسلجية وانها لاتؤمن بوجود مصادر معرفية خارج نطاق الواقع المنظور في حين قالت نظرية القرآن ان الانسان مكون من قسمين مادي (الجسم ) وروحي الهيولي ) ) وان المعرفة ظاهرة روحية حسية وفيها انواع من الفيض الالهي او المدد الغيبي وان الوجود الفكري لا ينحصر في الذهن فقط
٤ ) ان المعرفة في اغلب نظريات الفلاسفة احادية الجانب فهي افلاطون تذكر وعند لوك حس وعند ديكارت افكار فطرية وعند فرويد غرائز
١ الهيولي : لفظ يوناني بمعنى الأصل والمادة وفي الاصطلاح هي جوهر في الجسم قابل لما يعرض لذلك الجسم من الاتصال والانفصال محل للصورتين الجسمية، والنوعية. وبعبارة اوضح ان الهيولي هو جوهر روحي يتخذ شكل الانسان الخارجي نفسه ويمثل الجسم صورة مادية طبق الاصل للهيولي . وعند الموت يستخرج الهيولي الروحي ويبقى الجانب المادي من الهيولي وفي يوم القيامة يحدث التعاشق والتلاصق مرة اخرى انظر: التعريفات علي بن محمد الجرجاني (ت ٨١٦هـ ، ١٤١٣م ( القاهرة سنة ١٣٠٦هـ ، ۱۸۸۸م، ج۱ ص ۸۳
(2 Points of difference between the Quran and the theories of philosophers
(1) The theory of the Noble Quran differed from all sophistical sayings, for the Quran affirmed the existence of absolute truths about the universe, life, creation, and knowledge, while sophism denied that completely and in detail. We can derive a Quranic response to sophism from the blessed verse: 'And among the people is he who argues about Allah without knowledge, guidance, or an enlightening book.'
2) The Noble Quran differed with the theory of recollection regarding the concept of remembrance. The Quran said that humans in the afterlife remember the life before death, while Plato's theory said that humans remember from the world before birth. Plato's theory differed from the Wise Reminder in the nature of the information that humans remember: the Quran said humans remember their good or bad deeds done in life, while Plato's theory said humans remember the Forms. The term 'remembrance' also differed: Plato said remembrance is recovery, while the Wise Reminder intended preservation. Most philosophical theories studied in this research said: existence equals matter and knowledge is a material phenomenon realized through physical, chemical, and physiological interactions, and they do not believe in the existence of cognitive sources outside the scope of the visible reality. Meanwhile, the Quranic theory said that humans are composed of two parts: a material part (the body) and a spiritual part (hyle), and that knowledge is a spiritual-sensory phenomenon containing types of divine effusion or unseen supply, and that intellectual existence is not confined only to the mind.
4) In most philosophical theories, knowledge is one-sided: for Plato it is recollection, for Locke it is sense, for Descartes it is innate ideas, and for Freud it is instincts.
1 Hyle: a Greek word meaning origin and matter. In terminology, it is a substance in the body susceptible to what occurs to that body of connection and separation, a locus for the two forms: bodily and specific. In clearer terms, hyle is a spiritual substance that takes the form of the external human himself, and the body represents a material image identical to the hyle. At death, the spiritual hyle is extracted, and the material side of the hyle remains, and on the Day of Resurrection, attachment and adhesion occur again. See: al-Ta'rifat by Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jurjani (d. 816 AH, 1413 CE), Cairo 1306 AH, 1888 CE, vol. 1, p. 83.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage lists four points of difference between Quranic epistemology and philosophical theories. 
First difference: Against sophism, the Quran asserts absolute truths about existence, creation, and knowledge, whereas sophists deny objective truth. A Quranic verse (22:8) is cited as a response, highlighting arguments without knowledge. 
Second difference: Regarding recollection, the Quran and Plato differ. The Quran teaches that in the afterlife, humans remember their deeds from this life, while Plato claims recollection from a pre-birth world of Forms. The Quran views remembrance as preservation (hifz) of deeds, not recovery (istirja') of eternal Forms. 
Third difference: Materialist philosophies reduce existence to matter and knowledge to physical processes, while the Quran posits a dual nature: material body and spiritual hyle (a Greek term explained in the footnote). Knowledge is both spiritual and sensory, involving divine effusion (fayd ilahi) and unseen supply, and intellectual existence transcends the mind. 
Fourth difference: Most philosophical theories are one-sided, reducing knowledge to a single source (e.g., Plato: recollection, Locke: sense, Descartes: innate ideas, Freud: instincts), whereas the Quran offers a comprehensive view. The footnote defines hyle as a spiritual substance underlying the body, surviving death and reuniting at resurrection. The passage critically distinguishes the Quranic worldview from reductive philosophical systems.

Key Terms
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The existence of absolute truths about the universe
B) The value of sense perception
C) The existence of multiple gods
D) The afterlife

Key Takeaway
The Quran's epistemology is founded on absolute truths, directly opposing the sophist denial of objectivity.

Quranic Remembrance of Deeds vs Platonic Recollection of Forms
Second difference: Quran vs Plato on remembrance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Quranic concept of remembrance (of deeds in the afterlife) from Plato's theory of recollection (of pre-natal Forms) and explain the implications for human accountability.

Core Idea
The Quran teaches remembering deeds after death; Plato teaches remembering Forms from before birth.

Here we explore the second key difference: the nature of remembrance. The Quran speaks of humans in the afterlife remembering their deeds, while Plato's theory of anamnesis claims that learning is remembering eternal Forms from a prior existence. This difference has profound implications for the purpose of life and accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic view
What does the Quran say humans remember and when? (after death, their deeds)
The passage states 'the Quran said that humans in the afterlife remember the life before death' and 'remember their good or bad deeds'.
2
Identify Plato's view
What does Plato say humans remember and from when? (Forms, from pre-birth)
The passage states 'Plato said humans remember the Forms' from the world before birth.
3
Analyze the term 'remembrance'
How does the Quran's 'preservation' (hifz) differ from Plato's 'recovery' (istirja')?
The Quran intends preservation of deeds; Plato intends recovery of eternal Forms.
4
Consider the ethical implications
Why does remembering deeds matter for justice? It ensures accountability in the afterlife.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲ نقاط الاختلاف بين القرآن ونظريات الفلاسفة
(۱) اختلفت نظرية القرآن الكريم عن مقولات السفسطة كلها فالقرآن قال بوجود حقائق مطلقة عن الكون والحياة والخلق والمعرفة في حين نفت السوفسطائية ذلك جملة وتفصيلا . ويمكن ان نستلهم ردا قرآنيا على السفسطة في الآية المباركة (وَمِنَ النَّاسِ مَنْ يُجَادِلُ فِي اللهِ بِغَيْرِ عِلْمٍ وَلَا هُدًى وَلَا كِتَابٍ 
۲ ) اختلف القرآن الكريم مع نظرية الاستذكار حول مفهوم التذكر. فقال القرآن ان الانسان في حياة ما بعد الموت يتذكر حياة ما قبل الموت . في حين قالت نظرية افلاطون ان الانسان يتذكر من عالم ما قبل الولادة واختلفت نظرية افلاطون عن الذكر الحكيم في ماهية المعلومات التي يتذكرها الانسان فقال القرآن ان الانسان يتذكر اعماله الصالحة او الطالحة التي عملها في الحياة . قالت نظرية افلاطون ان الانسان يتذكر المثل . واختلف مصطلح التذكر فقد قال افلاطون ان التذكر استرجاع في حين قصد القرآن الكريم الحفظ . قالت اغلب نظريات الفلسفة التي درسناها في هذا البحث : ان الوجود يساوي المادة وان المعرفة ظاهرة مادية تتحقق اثر التعاملات الفيزيائية والكيميائية والفسلجية وانها لاتؤمن بوجود مصادر معرفية خارج نطاق الواقع المنظور في حين قالت نظرية القرآن ان الانسان مكون من قسمين مادي (الجسم ) وروحي الهيولي ) ) وان المعرفة ظاهرة روحية حسية وفيها انواع من الفيض الالهي او المدد الغيبي وان الوجود الفكري لا ينحصر في الذهن فقط
٤ ) ان المعرفة في اغلب نظريات الفلاسفة احادية الجانب فهي افلاطون تذكر وعند لوك حس وعند ديكارت افكار فطرية وعند فرويد غرائز
١ الهيولي : لفظ يوناني بمعنى الأصل والمادة وفي الاصطلاح هي جوهر في الجسم قابل لما يعرض لذلك الجسم من الاتصال والانفصال محل للصورتين الجسمية، والنوعية. وبعبارة اوضح ان الهيولي هو جوهر روحي يتخذ شكل الانسان الخارجي نفسه ويمثل الجسم صورة مادية طبق الاصل للهيولي . وعند الموت يستخرج الهيولي الروحي ويبقى الجانب المادي من الهيولي وفي يوم القيامة يحدث التعاشق والتلاصق مرة اخرى انظر: التعريفات علي بن محمد الجرجاني (ت ٨١٦هـ ، ١٤١٣م ( القاهرة سنة ١٣٠٦هـ ، ۱۸۸۸م، ج۱ ص ۸۳
(2 Points of difference between the Quran and the theories of philosophers
(1) The theory of the Noble Quran differed from all sophistical sayings, for the Quran affirmed the existence of absolute truths about the universe, life, creation, and knowledge, while sophism denied that completely and in detail. We can derive a Quranic response to sophism from the blessed verse: 'And among the people is he who argues about Allah without knowledge, guidance, or an enlightening book.'
2) The Noble Quran differed with the theory of recollection regarding the concept of remembrance. The Quran said that humans in the afterlife remember the life before death, while Plato's theory said that humans remember from the world before birth. Plato's theory differed from the Wise Reminder in the nature of the information that humans remember: the Quran said humans remember their good or bad deeds done in life, while Plato's theory said humans remember the Forms. The term 'remembrance' also differed: Plato said remembrance is recovery, while the Wise Reminder intended preservation. Most philosophical theories studied in this research said: existence equals matter and knowledge is a material phenomenon realized through physical, chemical, and physiological interactions, and they do not believe in the existence of cognitive sources outside the scope of the visible reality. Meanwhile, the Quranic theory said that humans are composed of two parts: a material part (the body) and a spiritual part (hyle), and that knowledge is a spiritual-sensory phenomenon containing types of divine effusion or unseen supply, and that intellectual existence is not confined only to the mind.
4) In most philosophical theories, knowledge is one-sided: for Plato it is recollection, for Locke it is sense, for Descartes it is innate ideas, and for Freud it is instincts.
1 Hyle: a Greek word meaning origin and matter. In terminology, it is a substance in the body susceptible to what occurs to that body of connection and separation, a locus for the two forms: bodily and specific. In clearer terms, hyle is a spiritual substance that takes the form of the external human himself, and the body represents a material image identical to the hyle. At death, the spiritual hyle is extracted, and the material side of the hyle remains, and on the Day of Resurrection, attachment and adhesion occur again. See: al-Ta'rifat by Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jurjani (d. 816 AH, 1413 CE), Cairo 1306 AH, 1888 CE, vol. 1, p. 83.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage lists four points of difference between Quranic epistemology and philosophical theories. 
First difference: Against sophism, the Quran asserts absolute truths about existence, creation, and knowledge, whereas sophists deny objective truth. A Quranic verse (22:8) is cited as a response, highlighting arguments without knowledge. 
Second difference: Regarding recollection, the Quran and Plato differ. The Quran teaches that in the afterlife, humans remember their deeds from this life, while Plato claims recollection from a pre-birth world of Forms. The Quran views remembrance as preservation (hifz) of deeds, not recovery (istirja') of eternal Forms. 
Third difference: Materialist philosophies reduce existence to matter and knowledge to physical processes, while the Quran posits a dual nature: material body and spiritual hyle (a Greek term explained in the footnote). Knowledge is both spiritual and sensory, involving divine effusion (fayd ilahi) and unseen supply, and intellectual existence transcends the mind. 
Fourth difference: Most philosophical theories are one-sided, reducing knowledge to a single source (e.g., Plato: recollection, Locke: sense, Descartes: innate ideas, Freud: instincts), whereas the Quran offers a comprehensive view. The footnote defines hyle as a spiritual substance underlying the body, surviving death and reuniting at resurrection. The passage critically distinguishes the Quranic worldview from reductive philosophical systems.

Key Terms
الاستذكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Plato claim humans remember?
A) Their good and bad deeds
B) The Forms (al-mithal)
C) Their past lives
D) Their instincts

Key Takeaway
The Quranic remembrance is a preservation of deeds for accountability, not a recovery of eternal Forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a short response explaining why the Quranic view of remembrance is more consistent with divine justice than Plato's theory.
?
Compare the Quranic term 'hifz' (preservation) with Platonic 'istirja'' (recovery) and discuss the implications for human responsibility.

The Quranic View of Human Nature and Knowledge vs Materialist Philosophy
Third difference: Quran vs materialist theories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Quranic dualism (body and spiritual hyle) with materialist monism, and explain how the Quran accounts for divine effusion in knowledge.

Core Idea
The Quran affirms a spiritual component (hyle) and divine sources of knowledge; materialism reduces everything to matter.

The third difference addresses the very nature of reality and knowledge. Most philosophical theories equate existence with matter and knowledge with physical processes. The Quran, however, posits a dual composition of humans—material body and spiritual hyle—and recognizes knowledge as both sensory and spiritual, involving divine effusion (fayd ilahi). This lesson unpacks that contrast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the materialist claim
What do most philosophical theories say about existence and knowledge? (existence = matter; knowledge is physical)
The passage states 'existence equals matter and knowledge is a material phenomenon realized through physical, chemical, and physiological interactions'.
2
Identify the Quranic claim
What does the Quran say about human composition? (body + spiritual hyle)
The passage says 'humans are composed of two parts: a material part (the body) and a spiritual part (hyle)'.
3
Examine the Quranic view of knowledge
How does it include divine effusion and unseen supply? Knowledge is a spiritual-sensory phenomenon with types of divine effusion.
4
Understand the term 'hyle'
Use the footnote to grasp the spiritual substance: a spiritual substance that takes the form of the external human, surviving death and reuniting at resurrection.
The footnote defines hyle as a spiritual substance, not prime matter.

The Quran's Comprehensive Epistemology vs One-Sided Philosophical Theories
Fourth difference: Quran vs one-sided theories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the reductionist nature of several philosophical theories of knowledge and contrast them with the Quran's multifaceted approach.

Core Idea
Most philosophers reduce knowledge to a single source; the Quran encompasses multiple sources.

The fourth and final difference highlights that most philosophical theories of knowledge are one-sided: Plato emphasizes recollection, Locke emphasizes sense, Descartes emphasizes innate ideas, and Freud emphasizes instincts. In contrast, the Quran does not limit knowledge to a single source but recognizes multiple pathways, including revelation, reason, sense, and spiritual insight.

Guided Path
1
List the one-sided theories
What are the examples given? (Plato: recollection, Locke: sense, Descartes: innate ideas, Freud: instincts)
The passage states 'for Plato it is recollection, for Locke it is sense, for Descartes it is innate ideas, and for Freud it is instincts'.
2
Identify the common flaw
How are they one-sided? They reduce knowledge to a single source.
3
State the Quranic alternative
What does the Quran offer? A comprehensive epistemology that includes multiple sources (revelation, reason, sense, spiritual insight).
4
Reflect on the implication
Why is a comprehensive epistemology important for Islamic thought? It allows integration of different ways of knowing.
