Commentary
This passage lists four points of difference between Quranic epistemology and philosophical theories. 
First difference: Against sophism, the Quran asserts absolute truths about existence, creation, and knowledge, whereas sophists deny objective truth. A Quranic verse (22:8) is cited as a response, highlighting arguments without knowledge. 
Second difference: Regarding recollection, the Quran and Plato differ. The Quran teaches that in the afterlife, humans remember their deeds from this life, while Plato claims recollection from a pre-birth world of Forms. The Quran views remembrance as preservation (hifz) of deeds, not recovery (istirja') of eternal Forms. 
Third difference: Materialist philosophies reduce existence to matter and knowledge to physical processes, while the Quran posits a dual nature: material body and spiritual hyle (a Greek term explained in the footnote). Knowledge is both spiritual and sensory, involving divine effusion (fayd ilahi) and unseen supply, and intellectual existence transcends the mind. 
Fourth difference: Most philosophical theories are one-sided, reducing knowledge to a single source (e.g., Plato: recollection, Locke: sense, Descartes: innate ideas, Freud: instincts), whereas the Quran offers a comprehensive view. The footnote defines hyle as a spiritual substance underlying the body, surviving death and reuniting at resurrection. The passage critically distinguishes the Quranic worldview from reductive philosophical systems.

Key Terms
السفسطة (al-safsata): Sophism; the philosophical position denying absolute truths and objective reality, which the Quran opposes. [Note: In classical logic, safsata refers to sophistical refutations; here it is used broadly for relativist epistemology.]
الاستذكار (al-istidhkar): Plato's theory of recollection (anamnesis), the idea that learning is remembering Forms from a previous existence.
الهيولي (al-hayula): Hyle; a spiritual substance that takes the form of the human body, distinct from the material body, surviving death and reattaching at resurrection. [Note: In Aristotelian philosophy, hyle means prime matter; here it is reinterpreted as a spiritual substance in Islamic philosophy.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a one-sided philosophical theory in the passage?
A) Plato: recollection
B) Locke: sense
C) Kant: categories
D) Freud: instincts

Key Takeaway
The Quran's epistemology is comprehensive, rejecting any single-source reductionism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List three sources of knowledge in Islam that are not mentioned in the philosophical theories above.
?

Quranic Memory vs Platonic Recollection
Comparison of memory theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Quranic concept of tadhakkur (remembrance of deeds) with Plato's anamnesis (recollection of Forms) and identify the epistemological implications.

Core Idea
The Quran redefines memory as preservation of deeds, not retrieval of pre-existent Forms.

This passage contrasts two fundamentally different accounts of what we remember and why. Understanding this contrast clarifies how Quranic epistemology grounds moral accountability in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two theories of memory
Find the mention of Quran's view on tadhakkur and Plato's theory of anamnesis. Note that they differ on timing and object.
2
Note the object of memory
The Quran says man remembers his deeds (good or bad) in the afterlife. Plato says man remembers the Forms from the world before birth.
3
Compare the nature of the memory act
Plato views memory as retrieval (istirja'), while the Quran views it as preservation (hifdh). This changes the purpose of memory: for accountability vs. education.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how each theory shapes views on human purpose: accountability vs. enlightenment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ ) اختلف القرآن الكريم مع نظرية الاستذكار حول مفهوم التذكر. فقال القرآن ان الانسان في حياة ما بعد الموت يتذكر حياة ما قبل الموت . في حين قالت نظرية افلاطون ان الانسان يتذكر من عالم ما قبل الولادة واختلفت نظرية افلاطون عن الذكر الحكيم في ماهية المعلومات التي يتذكرها الانسان فقال القرآن ان الانسان يتذكر اعماله الصالحة او الطالحة التي عملها في الحياة . قالت نظرية افلاطون ان الانسان يتذكر المثل . واختلف مصطلح التذكر فقد قال افلاطون ان التذكر استرجاع في حين قصد القرآن الكريم الحفظ . قالت اغلب نظريات الفلسفة التي درسناها في هذا البحث : ان الوجود يساوي المادة وان المعرفة ظاهرة مادية تتحقق اثر التعاملات الفيزيائية والكيميائية والفسلجية وانها لاتؤمن بوجود مصادر معرفية خارج نطاق الواقع المنظور في حين قالت نظرية القرآن ان الانسان مكون من قسمين مادي (الجسم ) وروحي الهيولي ) ) وان المعرفة ظاهرة روحية حسية وفيها انواع من الفيض الالهي او المدد الغيبي وان الوجود الفكري لا ينحصر في الذهن فقط
عند
٤ ) ان المعرفة في اغلب نظريات الفلاسفة احادية الجانب فهي افلاطون تذكر وعند لوك حس وعند ديكارت افكار فطرية وعند فرويد غرائز
١ الهيولي : لفظ يوناني بمعنى الأصل والمادة وفي الاصطلاح هي جوهر في الجسم قابل لما يعرض لذلك الجسم من الاتصال والانفصال محل للصورتين الجسمية، والنوعية. وبعبارة اوضح ان الهيولي هو جوهر روحي يتخذ شكل الانسان الخارجي نفسه ويمثل الجسم صورة مادية طبق الاصل للهيولي . وعند الموت يستخرج الهيولي الروحي ويبقى الجانب المادي من الهيولي وفي يوم القيامة يحدث التعاشق والتلاصق مرة اخرى انظر: التعريفات علي بن محمد الجرجاني (ت ٨١٦هـ ، ١٤١٣م ( القاهرة سنة ١٣٠٦هـ ، ۱۸۸۸م، ج۱ ص ۸۳
ضامرة وعند الاشراقيين نور وعند ماركس الواقع الموضوعي . في حين نجد القرآن الحكيم يتحدث عن منظومة متكاملة شاملة مترابطة متحركة متطورة تشمل القوى الداخلية والخارجية ومنها الحواس والعقل والوحي والبصائر والنفس والقلب والصدر والوراثة والفطرة والشياطين والالهام والميزان والذاكرة والتعليم والغرائز والفيض الالهي والهداية والنبوة والسعي الانساني والرؤيا المنامية والعرفان والجانب الوراثي
٥ ) اختلفت نظرية القرآن مع النظريات الحسية والجدلية والقوة الكامنة في تقدير قيمة الحس. ان هذه النظريات حصرت المعرفة بالحس وقالت انه السبيل الوحيد للمعرفة في حين قالت نظرية القرآن ان مرحلة الحس هي مرحلة تمهيدية لمعارف ارقى واعلى .
(٦ ان النظريات الحسية ربطت المعرفة بالمادة الدماغية والجهاز الدماغي المادي في حين قال القرآن ان المعرفة مجردة عن المادة الدماغية وان المادة الدماغية عبارة عن وسيلة اتصال لاغير
۷) اختلف مفهوم الفطرة بين القرآن وديكارت . فالفطرة على لسان القرآن والاحاديث النبوية هي الميل الذاتي الى الشيء من صميم الذات بلا حافز خارجي كالميل الى العدالة والعفة والزواج والرفعة والتفوق فهي ميول طبيعية يجدها الانسان في ذاته .اما الفطرة في مصطلح ديكارت فهي افكار طبيعية يجدها الانسان مزروعة في نفسه وتبدو في غاية الجلاء والوضوح كفكرة الله - وفكرة الحركة وفكرة الامتداد وفكرة النفس وكأنها لوازم للعقل البشري من دون ان يكون العقل مصدرا لها بل هو حامل لها وان مصدرها شيء غير ذاته وعقله.
2) The Noble Quran differed with the theory of recollection concerning the concept of memory. The Quran said that man in the life after death remembers the life before death. Whereas Plato's theory said that man remembers from the world before birth. And Plato's theory differed from the Wise Remembrance in the nature of the information that man remembers: the Quran said that man remembers his good or bad deeds that he did in life. Plato's theory said that man remembers the Forms. And the term 'memory' differed: Plato said that memory is retrieval, while the Noble Quran meant preservation. Most philosophical theories that we have studied in this research said that existence equals matter and that knowledge is a material phenomenon that occurs as a result of physical, chemical, and physiological interactions, and they do not believe in the existence of cognitive sources outside the scope of the visible reality. Whereas the Quran's theory said that man is composed of two parts: a material part (the body) and a spiritual part (hyle), and that knowledge is a spiritual-sensory phenomenon that includes types of divine emanation or unseen support, and that intellectual existence is not confined only to the mind. At [point] 4) That knowledge in most philosophical theories is one-sided: with Plato it is recollection, with Locke it is sense, with Descartes it is innate ideas, with Freud it is instincts. [Footnote 1: Hyle: a Greek word meaning origin and matter. In terminology, it is a substance in the body capable of what occurs to that body of connection and separation, a locus for both corporeal and specific forms. In clearer terms, hyle is a spiritual substance that takes the form of the external human self, and the body represents a material image identical to the hyle. At death, the spiritual hyle is extracted and the material aspect of the hyle remains, and on the Day of Resurrection, attachment and adhesion occur again. See: al-Ta'rifat by Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jurjani (d. 816 AH, 1413 CE), Cairo year 1306 AH, 1888 CE, vol. 1, p. 83.] ... weak, and with the Illuminationists it is light, and with Marx it is objective reality. Whereas we find the Wise Quran speaking about a comprehensive, integrated, interconnected, dynamic, evolving system that includes internal and external powers, including senses, intellect, revelation, insights, soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition (fitra), devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine emanation, guidance, prophecy, human effort, dream visions, gnosis, and the hereditary aspect. 5) The Quran's theory differed with sense theories, dialectical theories, and the latent power theory in evaluating the sense. These theories confined knowledge to the senses and said that it is the only path to knowledge, whereas the Quran's theory said that the sense stage is a preparatory stage for higher and loftier forms of knowledge. (6) The sense theories linked knowledge to brain matter and the material brain system, whereas the Quran said that knowledge is abstracted from brain matter and that brain matter is merely a means of communication. 7) The concept of innate disposition (fitra) differed between the Quran and Descartes. Fitra according to the Quran and prophetic hadith is the innate inclination towards something from the core of the self without an external stimulus, like the inclination towards justice, chastity, marriage, nobility, and excellence; they are natural tendencies that man finds in himself. As for fitra in Descartes' terminology, it is natural ideas that man finds implanted in his soul, appearing in utmost clarity and distinctness, like the idea of God, the idea of motion, the idea of extension, and the idea of the soul, as if they are necessary properties of the human mind without the mind being their source, but rather it is a carrier for them, and their source is something other than his self and his mind.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a comparative epistemological analysis between the Quran and various philosophical theories. The author systematically contrasts specific points. The passage begins by addressing the concept of memory (tadhakkur). The Quran holds that in the afterlife humans remember their earthly deeds, while Plato's theory of anamnesis claims that humans recollect Forms from a pre-natal existence. The Quranic term 'memory' implies preservation (hifdh), whereas Plato's is retrieval (istirja'). The author next notes that most philosophical theories equate existence with matter and view knowledge as a material byproduct of physical, chemical, and physiological processes, denying any non-material sources. In contrast, the Quran posits a dual composition of man (body and spiritual hyle) and sees knowledge as a spiritual-sensory phenomenon including divine emanation and unseen support. The footnote defines hyle as a spiritual substance akin to the soul. The author then argues that philosophical theories are one-sided: Plato emphasizes recollection, Locke sensation, Descartes innate ideas, Freud instincts, Illuminationists light, and Marx objective reality. The Quran offers a comprehensive system encompassing multiple sources: senses, intellect, revelation, soul, heart, fitra, etc. The next point critiques sense-based theories for confining knowledge to sensation; the Quran views sensation as a preparatory stage. The sixth point contrasts the materialist view linking knowledge to brain matter; the Quran sees knowledge as abstract and the brain as mere interface. Finally, fitra (innate disposition) is defined: in the Quran it is an innate inclination towards good, while for Descartes it is innate clear ideas like God, motion, etc., placed in the mind from an external source. The author maintains an objective comparative tone, highlighting the Quran's multi-faceted epistemology versus the reductionist tendencies of philosophical systems.

Key Terms
التذكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Quran say man remembers in the afterlife?
A) The Forms from the world of ideas.
B) His good and bad deeds from worldly life.
C) The innate ideas implanted by God.
D) Nothing; memory is annihilated at death.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic memory is preservation of personal deeds for accountability, not retrieval of pre-existent Forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Read Surah al-Naba' 78:23. Identify the word 'tadhakkur' (or equivalent). Explain how the Quranic memory differs from Plato's anamnesis based on this passage.
?
2. Write a short essay: How does the Quranic view of memory support the concept of moral responsibility in the afterlife?

The Quranic Fitra vs Descartes' Innate Ideas
Contrast between Quranic fitra and Cartesian innate ideas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the Quranic concept of fitra (innate inclination) from Descartes' innate ideas.

Core Idea
Fitra is an innate inclination towards moral good, while Descartes' innate ideas are clear concepts placed in the mind.

The final point in the passage contrasts two concepts of 'innateness'—Quranic fitra and Cartesian innate ideas. Understanding this difference prevents confusion when translating terms across philosophical traditions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the paragraph about fitra
Find the numbered point 7 in the Arabic. It defines Quranic fitra and contrasts it with Descartes' innate ideas.
2
Identify the Quranic definition
Fitra is described as an innate inclination (mayl) towards specific moral goods like justice, chastity, marriage, nobility, excellence, without external stimulus.
Note: it is an inclination, not a proposition.
3
Identify Descartes' definition
Innate ideas for Descartes are natural ideas (e.g., God, motion, extension, soul) that are clear and distinct, placed in the mind from an external source.
They are concepts, not inclinations.
4
Compare and contrast
Quranic fitra is a motivational disposition; Cartesian innate ideas are cognitive contents. Both are innate but differ in nature and source.
5
Consider implications
This distinction affects how we understand human nature: fitra leads to moral action, innate ideas provide foundational knowledge.

Commentary
This passage presents a comparative epistemological analysis between the Quran and various philosophical theories. The author systematically contrasts specific points. The passage begins by addressing the concept of memory (tadhakkur). The Quran holds that in the afterlife humans remember their earthly deeds, while Plato's theory of anamnesis claims that humans recollect Forms from a pre-natal existence. The Quranic term 'memory' implies preservation (hifdh), whereas Plato's is retrieval (istirja'). The author next notes that most philosophical theories equate existence with matter and view knowledge as a material byproduct of physical, chemical, and physiological processes, denying any non-material sources. In contrast, the Quran posits a dual composition of man (body and spiritual hyle) and sees knowledge as a spiritual-sensory phenomenon including divine emanation and unseen support. The footnote defines hyle as a spiritual substance akin to the soul. The author then argues that philosophical theories are one-sided: Plato emphasizes recollection, Locke sensation, Descartes innate ideas, Freud instincts, Illuminationists light, and Marx objective reality. The Quran offers a comprehensive system encompassing multiple sources: senses, intellect, revelation, soul, heart, fitra, etc. The next point critiques sense-based theories for confining knowledge to sensation; the Quran views sensation as a preparatory stage. The sixth point contrasts the materialist view linking knowledge to brain matter; the Quran sees knowledge as abstract and the brain as mere interface. Finally, fitra (innate disposition) is defined: in the Quran it is an innate inclination towards good, while for Descartes it is innate clear ideas like God, motion, etc., placed in the mind from an external source. The author maintains an objective comparative tone, highlighting the Quran's multi-faceted epistemology versus the reductionist tendencies of philosophical systems.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Quranic definition of fitra?
A) Innate ideas placed in the mind by God.
B) An innate inclination towards moral goods without external stimulus.
C) The ability to reason about universal truths.
D) The memory of primordial covenant.

Key Takeaway
Quranic fitra is an innate moral inclination, not a set of innate concepts like Descartes' innate ideas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Holistic Quranic Epistemology vs One-Sided Philosophical Theories
Contrast between holistic Quranic epistemology and one-sided philosophical theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic epistemological sources and contrast them with the reductionist approaches of philosophical theories.

Core Idea
The Quran presents a multi-faceted epistemology encompassing internal and external sources, unlike the one-sided theories of philosophers.

This passage contrasts the Quran's comprehensive system with the narrow focus of various philosophical schools. This lesson maps out the Quranic sources and shows how they integrate.

Guided Path
1
List the philosophical theories and their single source
From the passage: Plato (recollection), Locke (senses), Descartes (innate ideas), Freud (instincts), Illuminationists (light), Marx (objective reality). Identify one source each.
2
Extract the Quranic sources
Search the passage for the list: senses, intellect, revelation, insights, soul, heart, chest, heredity, fitra, devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine emanation, guidance, prophecy, human effort, dream visions, gnosis.
Note that even rejected sources like devils are included.
3
Categorize the Quranic sources
Group them into internal (e.g., soul, heart, fitra) and external (e.g., senses, revelation) sources. Also consider spiritual vs. material.
4
Assess the implications
Why does the Quran include such a wide range? This holistic view acknowledges multiple paths to knowledge and the complex nature of human cognition.

ضامرة وعند الاشراقيين نور وعند ماركس الواقع الموضوعي . في حين نجد القرآن الحكيم يتحدث عن منظومة متكاملة شاملة مترابطة متحركة متطورة تشمل القوى الداخلية والخارجية ومنها الحواس والعقل والوحي والبصائر والنفس والقلب والصدر والوراثة والفطرة والشياطين والالهام والميزان والذاكرة والتعليم والغرائز والفيض الالهي والهداية والنبوة والسعي الانساني والرؤيا المنامية والعرفان والجانب الوراثي
٥ ) اختلفت نظرية القرآن مع النظريات الحسية والجدلية والقوة الكامنة في تقدير قيمة الحس. ان هذه النظريات حصرت المعرفة بالحس وقالت انه السبيل الوحيد للمعرفة في حين قالت نظرية القرآن ان مرحلة الحس هي مرحلة تمهيدية لمعارف ارقى واعلى .
(٦ ان النظريات الحسية ربطت المعرفة بالمادة الدماغية والجهاز الدماغي المادي في حين قال القرآن ان المعرفة مجردة عن المادة الدماغية وان المادة الدماغية عبارة عن وسيلة اتصال لاغير
۷) اختلف مفهوم الفطرة بين القرآن وديكارت . فالفطرة على لسان القرآن والاحاديث النبوية هي الميل الذاتي الى الشيء من صميم الذات بلا حافز خارجي كالميل الى العدالة والعفة والزواج والرفعة والتفوق فهي ميول طبيعية يجدها الانسان في ذاته .اما الفطرة في مصطلح ديكارت فهي افكار طبيعية يجدها الانسان مزروعة في نفسه وتبدو في غاية الجلاء والوضوح كفكرة الله - وفكرة الحركة وفكرة الامتداد وفكرة النفس وكأنها لوازم للعقل البشري من دون ان يكون العقل مصدرا لها بل هو حامل لها وان مصدرها شيء غير ذاته وعقله.
2) The Noble Quran differed with the theory of recollection concerning the concept of memory. The Quran said that man in the life after death remembers the life before death. Whereas Plato's theory said that man remembers from the world before birth. And Plato's theory differed from the Wise Remembrance in the nature of the information that man remembers: the Quran said that man remembers his good or bad deeds that he did in life. Plato's theory said that man remembers the Forms. And the term 'memory' differed: Plato said that memory is retrieval, while the Noble Quran meant preservation. Most philosophical theories that we have studied in this research said that existence equals matter and that knowledge is a material phenomenon that occurs as a result of physical, chemical, and physiological interactions, and they do not believe in the existence of cognitive sources outside the scope of the visible reality. Whereas the Quran's theory said that man is composed of two parts: a material part (the body) and a spiritual part (hyle), and that knowledge is a spiritual-sensory phenomenon that includes types of divine emanation or unseen support, and that intellectual existence is not confined only to the mind. At [point] 4) That knowledge in most philosophical theories is one-sided: with Plato it is recollection, with Locke it is sense, with Descartes it is innate ideas, with Freud it is instincts. [Footnote 1: Hyle: a Greek word meaning origin and matter. In terminology, it is a substance in the body capable of what occurs to that body of connection and separation, a locus for both corporeal and specific forms. In clearer terms, hyle is a spiritual substance that takes the form of the external human self, and the body represents a material image identical to the hyle. At death, the spiritual hyle is extracted and the material aspect of the hyle remains, and on the Day of Resurrection, attachment and adhesion occur again. See: al-Ta'rifat by Ali ibn Muhammad al-Jurjani (d. 816 AH, 1413 CE), Cairo year 1306 AH, 1888 CE, vol. 1, p. 83.] ... weak, and with the Illuminationists it is light, and with Marx it is objective reality. Whereas we find the Wise Quran speaking about a comprehensive, integrated, interconnected, dynamic, evolving system that includes internal and external powers, including senses, intellect, revelation, insights, soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition (fitra), devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine emanation, guidance, prophecy, human effort, dream visions, gnosis, and the hereditary aspect. 5) The Quran's theory differed with sense theories, dialectical theories, and the latent power theory in evaluating the sense. These theories confined knowledge to the senses and said that it is the only path to knowledge, whereas the Quran's theory said that the sense stage is a preparatory stage for higher and loftier forms of knowledge. (6) The sense theories linked knowledge to brain matter and the material brain system, whereas the Quran said that knowledge is abstracted from brain matter and that brain matter is merely a means of communication. 7) The concept of innate disposition (fitra) differed between the Quran and Descartes. Fitra according to the Quran and prophetic hadith is the innate inclination towards something from the core of the self without an external stimulus, like the inclination towards justice, chastity, marriage, nobility, and excellence; they are natural tendencies that man finds in himself. As for fitra in Descartes' terminology, it is natural ideas that man finds implanted in his soul, appearing in utmost clarity and distinctness, like the idea of God, the idea of motion, the idea of extension, and the idea of the soul, as if they are necessary properties of the human mind without the mind being their source, but rather it is a carrier for them, and their source is something other than his self and his mind.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a comparative epistemological analysis between the Quran and various philosophical theories. The author systematically contrasts specific points. The passage begins by addressing the concept of memory (tadhakkur). The Quran holds that in the afterlife humans remember their earthly deeds, while Plato's theory of anamnesis claims that humans recollect Forms from a pre-natal existence. The Quranic term 'memory' implies preservation (hifdh), whereas Plato's is retrieval (istirja'). The author next notes that most philosophical theories equate existence with matter and view knowledge as a material byproduct of physical, chemical, and physiological processes, denying any non-material sources. In contrast, the Quran posits a dual composition of man (body and spiritual hyle) and sees knowledge as a spiritual-sensory phenomenon including divine emanation and unseen support. The footnote defines hyle as a spiritual substance akin to the soul. The author then argues that philosophical theories are one-sided: Plato emphasizes recollection, Locke sensation, Descartes innate ideas, Freud instincts, Illuminationists light, and Marx objective reality. The Quran offers a comprehensive system encompassing multiple sources: senses, intellect, revelation, soul, heart, fitra, etc. The next point critiques sense-based theories for confining knowledge to sensation; the Quran views sensation as a preparatory stage. The sixth point contrasts the materialist view linking knowledge to brain matter; the Quran sees knowledge as abstract and the brain as mere interface. Finally, fitra (innate disposition) is defined: in the Quran it is an innate inclination towards good, while for Descartes it is innate clear ideas like God, motion, etc., placed in the mind from an external source. The author maintains an objective comparative tone, highlighting the Quran's multi-faceted epistemology versus the reductionist tendencies of philosophical systems.

The Role of Sense Perception in Quranic Epistemology
Author enumerates differences between Quranic and philosophical theories of knowledge on sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Quranic view of sense perception as a preparatory stage with the philosophical view that confines knowledge to the senses.

Core Idea
Sense perception is a door, not the whole house.

In this passage, the author highlights a fundamental difference: sensory theories restrict knowledge to empirical observation, while the Quran treats sense as a preliminary stage leading to higher forms of knowledge. Understanding this distinction is key to appreciating the Quran's integrated epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical claim about sense
Locate where the author says philosophical theories confine knowledge to sense and consider it the only path to knowledge.
The text states: 'These theories confined knowledge to sense and said it is the only path to knowledge.'
2
Extract the Quranic counterclaim
Find where the Quran states that sense is a preparatory stage for higher knowledge.
The text says: 'the Quran's theory said that the stage of sense is a preparatory stage for higher and loftier knowledge.'
3
Note the additional point about brain matter
Sensory theories tie knowledge to brain matter, while the Quran abstracts knowledge from it, treating the brain as a mere communication means.
This reinforces the concept that knowledge is not reducible to physical processes.
4
Reflect on the implication
If sense is only preliminary, then what are the higher sources? The passage mentions revelation, insights, heart, etc., showing the Quran's multi-faceted epistemology.

Oppression, and according to the Illuminationists light, and according to Marx objective reality. While we find the Wise Quran speaks about an integrated, comprehensive, interconnected, dynamic, evolving system that includes internal and external powers, among them senses, intellect, revelation, insights, soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition, devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine effusion, guidance, prophethood, human striving, dream vision, gnosis, and the hereditary aspect. 5) The Quran's theory differed from sensory, dialectical, and potential force theories in evaluating the sense. These theories confined knowledge to sense and said it is the only path to knowledge, while the Quran's theory said that the stage of sense is a preparatory stage for higher and loftier knowledge. (6) The sensory theories linked knowledge to brain matter and the material brain system, while the Quran said knowledge is abstracted from brain matter and that brain matter is merely a means of communication. 7) The concept of innate disposition (fitra) differed between the Quran and Descartes. Fitra according to the Quran and prophetic hadiths is the essential inclination towards something from the core of the self without external stimulus, like the inclination towards justice, chastity, marriage, elevation, and excellence; these are natural inclinations that a person finds in themselves. As for fitra in Descartes's terminology, it is natural ideas that a person finds planted in their self and appear in utmost clarity and obviousness, such as the idea of God, the idea of motion, the idea of extension, and the idea of the self, as if they are necessities of the human mind without the mind being their source, rather it is their carrier and their source is something other than its self and its mind. 8) There is also a difference concerning the primary rational principles between the Quran and the theories of innate ideas and potential force: while Descartes's theory called them innate ideas and the potential force theory called them primary principles, the Quran's theory called them insights (basa'ir). 9) The Quran, unlike all philosophical theories, gave a specialized center to emotional or affective knowledge and called it the heart - as a metaphor for conscience - while the subject of emotions was marginal and fluctuating in the theories of philosophers who treated the human mind as if it were a computer that takes and gives without having emotions such as sadness, joy, love, hatred, and others. 10) The Quran's theory of knowledge gave special privilege and preference to hearing and sight, while philosophical theories treated the tools of sense equally. And there was a conflict between the potential force theory, which preferred sight over hearing, while the Quran preferred hearing over sight. 11) The verses of the Quran spoke about the chest (sadr) and considered it a container for much information and grudges, while philosophical books did not refer to this aspect either closely or remotely. 12) Philosophical theories treated the tools of knowledge as a mechanical machine, while the Quran treated the tools of knowledge as a living being that takes and gives, desires and fears. It connects with the upper world - the unseen - and connects with the lower world - experiences - in a mode of influence and being influenced.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of epistemological theories, contrasting the Quranic view with various philosophical doctrines. The author begins by summarizing philosophical positions: 'oppression' (perhaps referring to a materialist view), Illuminationist light, and Marx's objective reality. Then he introduces the Quranic concept of a comprehensive system encompassing internal and external powers: senses, intellect, revelation, insights (basa'ir), soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition (fitra), devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine effusion, guidance, prophethood, human striving, dream vision, gnosis, and hereditary aspects. This list establishes the Quran's multi-faceted epistemology. Point 5: The Quran differs from sensualist, dialectical, and potential-force theories in valuing sense. Those theories confine knowledge to sense; the Quran sees sense as preliminary to higher knowledge. Point 6: Sensory theories tie knowledge to brain matter; the Quran treats knowledge as abstract and the brain as a mere conduit. Point 7: The concept of fitra differs: Quranic fitra is an intrinsic inclination (e.g., toward justice, chastity), while Descartes's fitra is innate ideas (e.g., God, motion) that the mind carries but does not originate. Point 8: Primary principles: Descartes calls them innate ideas, potential-force theory calls them first principles, the Quran calls them basa'ir (insights). Point 9: The Quran gives a specialized role to the heart (metaphor for conscience) for emotional knowledge, whereas philosophers treat the mind as a computer devoid of emotions. Point 10: The Quran privileges hearing and sight, with hearing preferred over sight, contrary to some philosophical theories that treat senses equally or prefer sight. Point 11: The Quran mentions the chest (sadr) as a receptacle for information and grudges, absent in philosophical works. Point 12: Philosophers see cognitive tools as mechanical; the Quran sees them as living entities interacting with the unseen and experiential worlds in a dynamic exchange.

المعرفة

Fitra: Quranic Innate Inclination vs Cartesian Innate Ideas
Author compares Quranic and Cartesian concepts of fitra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Quranic concept of fitra as an innate inclination and Descartes' concept of innate ideas.

Core Idea
Fitra is a pull from within, not a planted concept.

The term 'fitra' appears in both Islamic and Western philosophical traditions but with strikingly different meanings. This passage clarifies the Quranic view: fitra is an essential inclination towards certain virtues, unlike Descartes' innate ideas which are pre-installed concepts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic definition of fitra
Find where the text defines fitra as 'essential inclination towards something from the core of the self without external stimulus.'
The text explicitly contrasts this with Descartes' definition.
2
Identify examples of Quranic fitra
The text mentions inclinations towards justice, chastity, marriage, elevation, and excellence as examples.
These are natural tendencies people find within themselves.
3
Find Descartes' definition
Descartes' fitra is described as natural ideas planted in the self, clear and obvious, such as God, motion, extension, self.
Note: for Descartes, the mind is a carrier, not the source.
4
Contrast the two concepts
Quranic fitra is an inclination (a tendency); Cartesian innate ideas are propositions or concepts. Also note the source: Quranic fitra originates from the self; Descartes' ideas have an external source.

ضامرة وعند الاشراقيين نور وعند ماركس الواقع الموضوعي . في حين نجد القرآن الحكيم يتحدث عن منظومة متكاملة شاملة مترابطة متحركة متطورة تشمل القوى الداخلية والخارجية ومنها الحواس والعقل والوحي والبصائر والنفس والقلب والصدر والوراثة والفطرة والشياطين والالهام والميزان والذاكرة والتعليم والغرائز والفيض الالهي والهداية والنبوة والسعي الانساني والرؤيا المنامية والعرفان والجانب الوراثي . ٥ ) اختلفت نظرية القرآن مع النظريات الحسية والجدلية والقوة الكامنة في تقدير قيمة الحس. ان هذه النظريات حصرت المعرفة بالحس وقالت انه السبيل الوحيد للمعرفة في حين قالت نظرية القرآن ان مرحلة الحس هي مرحلة تمهيدية لمعارف ارقى واعلى . (٦ ان النظريات الحسية ربطت المعرفة بالمادة الدماغية والجهاز الدماغي المادي في حين قال القرآن ان المعرفة مجردة عن المادة الدماغية وان المادة الدماغية عبارة عن وسيلة اتصال لاغير ۷) اختلف مفهوم الفطرة بين القرآن وديكارت . فالفطرة على لسان القرآن والاحاديث النبوية هي الميل الذاتي الى الشيء من صميم الذات بلا حافز خارجي كالميل الى العدالة والعفة والزواج والرفعة والتفوق فهي ميول طبيعية يجدها الانسان في ذاته .اما الفطرة في مصطلح ديكارت فهي افكار طبيعية يجدها الانسان مزروعة في نفسه وتبدو في غاية الجلاء والوضوح كفكرة الله - وفكرة الحركة وفكرة الامتداد وفكرة النفس وكأنها لوازم للعقل البشري من دون ان يكون العقل مصدرا لها بل هو حامل لها وان مصدرها شيء غير ذاته وعقله. ۸ ) ويوجد اختلاف حول المبادىء العقلية الاولية بين القرآن الكريم ونظريتي الافكار الفطرية والقوة الكامنة ففي حين سمتها نظرية ديكارت افكارا فطرية واسمتها نظرية القوة الكامنة المباديء الاولية سمتها نظرية القرآن بصائر. ۹ ) اعطى القرآن الكريم و بخلاف جميع نظريات الفلسفة مركزا تخصصيا للمعارف الوجدانية او العاطفية وسماه القلب - كناية عن الضمير - في حين كان موضوع الوجدانيات هامشيا متذبذبا في نظريات الفلاسفة الذين تعاملوا مع العقل البشري كأنه كمبيوتر يأخذ ويعطي من دون ان يكون له مشاعر مثل الحزن والفرح والحب والبغض وغيرها . ١٠ ) ان نظرية القرآن في المعرفة اعطت امتيازا وتفضيلا خاصا للسمع والبصر في حين تعاملت نظريات الفلاسفة مع ادوات الحس بالتساوي. وحدث تعارض بين نظرية القوة الكامنة التي فضلت البصر على السمع في حين ان القرآن الكريم فضل السمع على البصر (۱۱) تحدثت آيات القرآن الكريم عن الصدر وعدته وعاءا لكثير من المعلومات والضغائن في حين لم تشير كتب الفلسفة الى هذا الجانب من قريب او بعيده ۱۲ ) ان نظريات الفلاسفة تعاملت مع ادوات المعرفة كآلة ميكانيكية في حين تعامل القرآن الكريم مع ادوات المعرفة ككائن حي يأخذ ويعطي يرغب ويخاف . يتصل مع العالم العلوي - الغيب - ويتصل مع العالم السفلي- التجارب - بصيغة التأثر والتأثير .
Oppression, and according to the Illuminationists light, and according to Marx objective reality. While we find the Wise Quran speaks about an integrated, comprehensive, interconnected, dynamic, evolving system that includes internal and external powers, among them senses, intellect, revelation, insights, soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition, devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine effusion, guidance, prophethood, human striving, dream vision, gnosis, and the hereditary aspect. 5) The Quran's theory differed from sensory, dialectical, and potential force theories in evaluating the sense. These theories confined knowledge to sense and said it is the only path to knowledge, while the Quran's theory said that the stage of sense is a preparatory stage for higher and loftier knowledge. (6) The sensory theories linked knowledge to brain matter and the material brain system, while the Quran said knowledge is abstracted from brain matter and that brain matter is merely a means of communication. 7) The concept of innate disposition (fitra) differed between the Quran and Descartes. Fitra according to the Quran and prophetic hadiths is the essential inclination towards something from the core of the self without external stimulus, like the inclination towards justice, chastity, marriage, elevation, and excellence; these are natural inclinations that a person finds in themselves. As for fitra in Descartes's terminology, it is natural ideas that a person finds planted in their self and appear in utmost clarity and obviousness, such as the idea of God, the idea of motion, the idea of extension, and the idea of the self, as if they are necessities of the human mind without the mind being their source, rather it is their carrier and their source is something other than its self and its mind. 8) There is also a difference concerning the primary rational principles between the Quran and the theories of innate ideas and potential force: while Descartes's theory called them innate ideas and the potential force theory called them primary principles, the Quran's theory called them insights (basa'ir). 9) The Quran, unlike all philosophical theories, gave a specialized center to emotional or affective knowledge and called it the heart - as a metaphor for conscience - while the subject of emotions was marginal and fluctuating in the theories of philosophers who treated the human mind as if it were a computer that takes and gives without having emotions such as sadness, joy, love, hatred, and others. 10) The Quran's theory of knowledge gave special privilege and preference to hearing and sight, while philosophical theories treated the tools of sense equally. And there was a conflict between the potential force theory, which preferred sight over hearing, while the Quran preferred hearing over sight. 11) The verses of the Quran spoke about the chest (sadr) and considered it a container for much information and grudges, while philosophical books did not refer to this aspect either closely or remotely. 12) Philosophical theories treated the tools of knowledge as a mechanical machine, while the Quran treated the tools of knowledge as a living being that takes and gives, desires and fears. It connects with the upper world - the unseen - and connects with the lower world - experiences - in a mode of influence and being influenced.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of epistemological theories, contrasting the Quranic view with various philosophical doctrines. The author begins by summarizing philosophical positions: 'oppression' (perhaps referring to a materialist view), Illuminationist light, and Marx's objective reality. Then he introduces the Quranic concept of a comprehensive system encompassing internal and external powers: senses, intellect, revelation, insights (basa'ir), soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition (fitra), devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine effusion, guidance, prophethood, human striving, dream vision, gnosis, and hereditary aspects. This list establishes the Quran's multi-faceted epistemology. Point 5: The Quran differs from sensualist, dialectical, and potential-force theories in valuing sense. Those theories confine knowledge to sense; the Quran sees sense as preliminary to higher knowledge. Point 6: Sensory theories tie knowledge to brain matter; the Quran treats knowledge as abstract and the brain as a mere conduit. Point 7: The concept of fitra differs: Quranic fitra is an intrinsic inclination (e.g., toward justice, chastity), while Descartes's fitra is innate ideas (e.g., God, motion) that the mind carries but does not originate. Point 8: Primary principles: Descartes calls them innate ideas, potential-force theory calls them first principles, the Quran calls them basa'ir (insights). Point 9: The Quran gives a specialized role to the heart (metaphor for conscience) for emotional knowledge, whereas philosophers treat the mind as a computer devoid of emotions. Point 10: The Quran privileges hearing and sight, with hearing preferred over sight, contrary to some philosophical theories that treat senses equally or prefer sight. Point 11: The Quran mentions the chest (sadr) as a receptacle for information and grudges, absent in philosophical works. Point 12: Philosophers see cognitive tools as mechanical; the Quran sees them as living entities interacting with the unseen and experiential worlds in a dynamic exchange.

Key Terms
الفطرة
ديكارت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Quranic fitra?
A) Natural ideas like God and motion
B) An essential inclination towards something without external stimulus
C) A set of clear and obvious propositions
D) A concept identical to Descartes' innate ideas

Key Takeaway
The Quranic fitra is an innate pull towards certain values, not a set of pre-existing ideas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the idea of justice. According to Quranic fitra, is it an innate idea or an innate inclination? Explain using the passage.
?
If someone says 'The Quran supports Descartes' innate ideas,' what would you point out from this passage to correct them?

intermediate

Primary Principles: Basa'ir vs Innate Ideas vs First Principles
Author contrasts the Quranic term 'basa'ir' with philosophical terms for primary principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic term 'basa'ir' for primary rational principles and contrast it with Descartes' 'innate ideas' and the potential force theory's 'first principles'.

Core Idea
The Quran calls primary principles 'basa'ir' — insights that illuminate.

Different epistemological theories have different names for the most basic principles of reason. The passage shows that the Quran uses 'basa'ir' while Western theories use 'innate ideas' or 'first principles'. This is not just terminological but reflects different understandings of their nature and origin.

Guided Path
1
Find the three terms
Descartes says innate ideas, potential force theory says primary principles, Quran says basa'ir. Locate these in the text.
All refer to fundamental rational truths.
2
Understand the common referent
These are all references to the same kind of thing: basic truths that underlie all reasoning.
3
Note the Quran's choice of 'basa'ir'
The word 'basa'ir' implies illumination and clarity, suggesting these principles are insights given by God.
The term is part of the Quranic comprehensive system listed earlier.
4
Reflect on the implications
Calling them basa'ir highlights their revelatory and guidance-oriented nature, unlike Descartes' premise that they are merely innate.
This affects how one views the source of knowledge: God-given vs. inherent.

Heart and Chest: Quranic Centers for Emotional and Cognitive Knowledge
Author highlights Quranic concepts of heart and chest as epistemological centers absent in philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic concepts of heart (for emotional knowledge) and chest (as a container) and contrast them with philosophical neglect of such centers.

Core Idea
The Quran gives emotions a home; philosophy leaves them homeless.

While philosophical theories treat the mind as a purely rational computer, the Quran assigns specific roles to the heart (emotional knowledge) and the chest (storage of information and grudges). This lesson explores these unique Quranic contributions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quran's claim about the heart
Find where the text says the Quran gave a specialized center for emotional knowledge called the heart (qalb).
The heart is a metaphor for conscience.
2
Note the contrast with philosophers
Philosophical theories treat the mind as a computer without emotions; emotions are marginal.
3
Find the mention of the chest (sadr)
The Quran considers the chest a container for information and grudges; philosophy does not mention it.
This shows the Quran's holistic view of human cognition.
4
Consider the implications
The Quran's epistemology integrates emotion and cognition, while philosophy separates or ignores emotions.

Oppression, and according to the Illuminationists light, and according to Marx objective reality. While we find the Wise Quran speaks about an integrated, comprehensive, interconnected, dynamic, evolving system that includes internal and external powers, among them senses, intellect, revelation, insights, soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition, devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine effusion, guidance, prophethood, human striving, dream vision, gnosis, and the hereditary aspect. 5) The Quran's theory differed from sensory, dialectical, and potential force theories in evaluating the sense. These theories confined knowledge to sense and said it is the only path to knowledge, while the Quran's theory said that the stage of sense is a preparatory stage for higher and loftier knowledge. (6) The sensory theories linked knowledge to brain matter and the material brain system, while the Quran said knowledge is abstracted from brain matter and that brain matter is merely a means of communication. 7) The concept of innate disposition (fitra) differed between the Quran and Descartes. Fitra according to the Quran and prophetic hadiths is the essential inclination towards something from the core of the self without external stimulus, like the inclination towards justice, chastity, marriage, elevation, and excellence; these are natural inclinations that a person finds in themselves. As for fitra in Descartes's terminology, it is natural ideas that a person finds planted in their self and appear in utmost clarity and obviousness, such as the idea of God, the idea of motion, the idea of extension, and the idea of the self, as if they are necessities of the human mind without the mind being their source, rather it is their carrier and their source is something other than its self and its mind. 8) There is also a difference concerning the primary rational principles between the Quran and the theories of innate ideas and potential force: while Descartes's theory called them innate ideas and the potential force theory called them primary principles, the Quran's theory called them insights (basa'ir). 9) The Quran, unlike all philosophical theories, gave a specialized center to emotional or affective knowledge and called it the heart - as a metaphor for conscience - while the subject of emotions was marginal and fluctuating in the theories of philosophers who treated the human mind as if it were a computer that takes and gives without having emotions such as sadness, joy, love, hatred, and others. 10) The Quran's theory of knowledge gave special privilege and preference to hearing and sight, while philosophical theories treated the tools of sense equally. And there was a conflict between the potential force theory, which preferred sight over hearing, while the Quran preferred hearing over sight. 11) The verses of the Quran spoke about the chest (sadr) and considered it a container for much information and grudges, while philosophical books did not refer to this aspect either closely or remotely. 12) Philosophical theories treated the tools of knowledge as a mechanical machine, while the Quran treated the tools of knowledge as a living being that takes and gives, desires and fears. It connects with the upper world - the unseen - and connects with the lower world - experiences - in a mode of influence and being influenced.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of epistemological theories, contrasting the Quranic view with various philosophical doctrines. The author begins by summarizing philosophical positions: 'oppression' (perhaps referring to a materialist view), Illuminationist light, and Marx's objective reality. Then he introduces the Quranic concept of a comprehensive system encompassing internal and external powers: senses, intellect, revelation, insights (basa'ir), soul, heart, chest, heredity, innate disposition (fitra), devils, inspiration, balance, memory, education, instincts, divine effusion, guidance, prophethood, human striving, dream vision, gnosis, and hereditary aspects. This list establishes the Quran's multi-faceted epistemology. Point 5: The Quran differs from sensualist, dialectical, and potential-force theories in valuing sense. Those theories confine knowledge to sense; the Quran sees sense as preliminary to higher knowledge. Point 6: Sensory theories tie knowledge to brain matter; the Quran treats knowledge as abstract and the brain as a mere conduit. Point 7: The concept of fitra differs: Quranic fitra is an intrinsic inclination (e.g., toward justice, chastity), while Descartes's fitra is innate ideas (e.g., God, motion) that the mind carries but does not originate. Point 8: Primary principles: Descartes calls them innate ideas, potential-force theory calls them first principles, the Quran calls them basa'ir (insights). Point 9: The Quran gives a specialized role to the heart (metaphor for conscience) for emotional knowledge, whereas philosophers treat the mind as a computer devoid of emotions. Point 10: The Quran privileges hearing and sight, with hearing preferred over sight, contrary to some philosophical theories that treat senses equally or prefer sight. Point 11: The Quran mentions the chest (sadr) as a receptacle for information and grudges, absent in philosophical works. Point 12: Philosophers see cognitive tools as mechanical; the Quran sees them as living entities interacting with the unseen and experiential worlds in a dynamic exchange.

الصدر

?

Innate Desires and Moral Foundations: Quran vs Philosophy
Author contrasts Quranic vs philosophical views on desires and morality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Quranic and philosophical views on innate human desires and the source of moral values, identifying the key epistemological differences.

Core Idea
The Quran grounds morality in fixed divine truths; philosophy reduces it to mere accident.

This lesson examines two fundamental epistemological differences between the Quranic theory and philosophical theories of knowledge. First, the purpose of latent desires: does man innately yearn for goodness and truth, or merely for more knowledge? Second, the status of moral values: are they absolute axioms or relative outcomes? Understanding these contrasts reveals the Quran's unique integration of ethics and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic claim about latent desires
Locate the passage that states man has innate latent desires for love of good, justice, and truth. Write down the Quranic position in one sentence.
2
Identify the philosophical counterclaim
Find the passage that describes the theory of latent power: it claims that the latent desire is merely a desire for knowledge and increase. Contrast this with the Quranic view.
3
Compare the treatment of moral values
Read the point about moral values. The Quran treats them as fixed axioms and rational self-evident truths; philosophy treats them as accidental outcomes. Explain why this difference matters for epistemology.
4
Summarize the core epistemological divide
In a few sentences, summarize how the Quranic view provides a stable foundation for ethics and human purpose, while the philosophical view risks moral relativism.

19) The Holy Quran differed with the theory of latent power regarding the purpose of latent desires. The Quran said that in man there are innate, latent desires for love of good, justice, and truth. Meanwhile the theory of latent power said that it is a desire for knowledge and increase from time to time. (20) The Quranic theory indicated the condition of some people whose memory and mental centers weaken at the end of life, whereas all philosophical theories did not pay attention to this condition. (21) The Quranic theory gave special knowledge to a specific group of people, namely the prophets, saints, the Prophet's family, and his descendants, whereas we find philosophical theories speak about general concepts common to all people. (22) Philosophical theories tried to evade the subject of memory and its concept and failed to explain the issue of remembering and forgetting, whereas we find the Quranic theory gave specific answers to this issue and stripped it of matter. (23) The Quranic theory spoke about the secret of discoveries and inventions and said that a divine flash like light enters the human mind so he perceives the secrets of nature, and said that invention, for example, is a mixture of human effort and divine success, whereas philosophical theories did not speak about this light at all, except for a shy admission by the philosopher Descartes. 24) The Quranic theory dealt with moral values as fixed axioms and rational self-evident truths, whereas most philosophical theories dealt with moral values as accidental outcomes of knowledge. 25) The Quranic theory spoke about the inheritance of negative and positive traits, such as the inheritance of the bad side (as in the case of the Children of Israel) and the inheritance of the positive side (as in the inheritance of the family of Yasin), whereas philosophical theories of knowledge did not pay attention to this aspect at all. 26) The Quranic theory stated the existence of destructive and evil elements that affect the decision taken by man, namely the devils, whereas philosophical theories considered the subjects of good and evil as relative matters that change according to temporal and spatial conditions. 26) The Quranic theory stated that madness is the domination of devils over the soul, leading to the collapse of the system of inner perception, and philosophical theories did not touch on this subject at all. Philosophers neglected this important epistemological subject and referred it to psychology, which escaped from treating or explaining it, and transferred the subject again to psychiatry, which contented itself with placing sedative or narcotic treatments. 27) Language in philosophical theories is formed through experience, training, and need, whereas in the Quranic theory its first basic vocabulary is inspiration from God (And He taught Adam the names, all of them) (He taught him the explanation). 28) The Holy Quran differed with philosophical theories on the subject of dream visions. While the Quran said they are an important source of knowledge, philosophical theories said that dream visions are psychological manifestations with no value, and some said...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a series of comparative points between the Quranic theory of knowledge and various philosophical theories. The author argues for the superiority of the Quranic view by highlighting areas where philosophy falls short. The passage begins by contrasting the purpose of latent desires: the Quran sees innate longing for goodness and truth, while the theory of latent power sees a generic desire for more knowledge. Next, the Quranic theory accounts for cognitive decline in old age, which philosophy ignores. It then asserts that special knowledge is granted to prophets and saints, whereas philosophy posits universal concepts. Philosophy is criticized for evading the nature of memory, while the Quran provides material-free explanations. The origin of discoveries is attributed to a divine light in the Quran, with only Descartes hinting at it. Moral values are treated as absolute axioms in the Quran, but as relative outcomes in philosophy. The inheritance of traits, both good and bad, is recognized by the Quran (citing the examples of the Children of Israel and the family of Yasin), while philosophy ignores it. The existence of devils influencing human decisions is affirmed by the Quran, but philosophy reduces good and evil to relativity. Madness is explained as demonic possession in the Quran, whereas philosophy relegates it to psychology and psychiatry without real explanation. Language is divinely inspired according to the Quran (referencing the teaching of Adam), while philosophy sees it as empirical. Finally, dream visions are considered valuable sources of knowledge in the Quran, but dismissed as meaningless by philosophers. Each point illustrates the author's claim that the Quranic theory is more comprehensive and addresses realities overlooked by philosophy.

نظريات الفلاسفة
المعرفة

C) Both see them as relative.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic theory grounds morality in fixed truths, while philosophy treats it as relative—a foundational epistemological divergence.

Task: Identify a contemporary ethical issue (e.g., justice in wealth distribution) and contrast how a Quranic epistemologist and a philosophical empiricist might approach it.

Divine Selection and Inspiration in Knowledge
Author highlights Quranic emphasis on divinely bestowed knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Quranic concept of specially bestowed knowledge (prophets, saints) with the philosophical idea of universal rational knowledge, and explain the role of divine inspiration in language and invention.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not only earned but gifted—prophets and saints receive special insight, and language itself is divinely taught.

Philosophical theories often assume that knowledge is universally accessible through reason and experience. The Quranic theory, however, identifies a special category of knowledge granted to prophets, saints, and the Prophet's household. It also traces the origin of language to divine teaching and explains inventions as a blend of human effort and divine flash. This lesson unpacks those contrasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about special knowledge
Find the point where the author says the Quran gives special knowledge to a specific group: prophets, saints, etc. Note how this differs from philosophical universality.
2
Examine the source of language
Read point 27. The Quran says language is inspired (reference to Adam). Philosophy says it comes from experience. Write a sentence comparing the two origins.
3
Analyze the explanation of inventions
Point 23 describes a divine flash. Compare this with philosophy's silence (except Descartes). What does this imply about the sources of creativity?
4
Synthesize the role of divine intervention
Based on these points, explain why the Quranic theory sees knowledge as partly a gift, not merely an acquisition. How does this affect the concept of human potential?

تفسيره وحول الموضوع مرة اخرى الى الطب النفسي الذي اكتفى بوضع العلاجات المسكنة أو المخدرة. ۲۷ ) اللغة في نظريات الفلاسفة تتكون من خلال الخبرة والتدريب والحاجة أما في نظرية القرآن فان مفرداتها الاساسية الاولى هي الهام من الله (وعلم آدم الاسماء كلها) (علمه البيان) ۲۸ ) اختلف القرآن الكريم حول موضوع الرؤيا المنامية مع نظريات الفلاسفة ففي حين قال القرآن انها مصدر مهم من مصادر المعرفة قالت نظريات الفلاسفة ان الرؤيا المنامية هي تجليات نفسية لاقيمة لها وقال بعضهم
19) The Holy Quran differed with the theory of latent power regarding the purpose of latent desires. The Quran said that in man there are innate, latent desires for love of good, justice, and truth. Meanwhile the theory of latent power said that it is a desire for knowledge and increase from time to time. (20) The Quranic theory indicated the condition of some people whose memory and mental centers weaken at the end of life, whereas all philosophical theories did not pay attention to this condition. (21) The Quranic theory gave special knowledge to a specific group of people, namely the prophets, saints, the Prophet's family, and his descendants, whereas we find philosophical theories speak about general concepts common to all people. (22) Philosophical theories tried to evade the subject of memory and its concept and failed to explain the issue of remembering and forgetting, whereas we find the Quranic theory gave specific answers to this issue and stripped it of matter. (23) The Quranic theory spoke about the secret of discoveries and inventions and said that a divine flash like light enters the human mind so he perceives the secrets of nature, and said that invention, for example, is a mixture of human effort and divine success, whereas philosophical theories did not speak about this light at all, except for a shy admission by the philosopher Descartes. 24) The Quranic theory dealt with moral values as fixed axioms and rational self-evident truths, whereas most philosophical theories dealt with moral values as accidental outcomes of knowledge. 25) The Quranic theory spoke about the inheritance of negative and positive traits, such as the inheritance of the bad side (as in the case of the Children of Israel) and the inheritance of the positive side (as in the inheritance of the family of Yasin), whereas philosophical theories of knowledge did not pay attention to this aspect at all. 26) The Quranic theory stated the existence of destructive and evil elements that affect the decision taken by man, namely the devils, whereas philosophical theories considered the subjects of good and evil as relative matters that change according to temporal and spatial conditions. 26) The Quranic theory stated that madness is the domination of devils over the soul, leading to the collapse of the system of inner perception, and philosophical theories did not touch on this subject at all. Philosophers neglected this important epistemological subject and referred it to psychology, which escaped from treating or explaining it, and transferred the subject again to psychiatry, which contented itself with placing sedative or narcotic treatments. 27) Language in philosophical theories is formed through experience, training, and need, whereas in the Quranic theory its first basic vocabulary is inspiration from God (And He taught Adam the names, all of them) (He taught him the explanation). 28) The Holy Quran differed with philosophical theories on the subject of dream visions. While the Quran said they are an important source of knowledge, philosophical theories said that dream visions are psychological manifestations with no value, and some said...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a series of comparative points between the Quranic theory of knowledge and various philosophical theories. The author argues for the superiority of the Quranic view by highlighting areas where philosophy falls short. The passage begins by contrasting the purpose of latent desires: the Quran sees innate longing for goodness and truth, while the theory of latent power sees a generic desire for more knowledge. Next, the Quranic theory accounts for cognitive decline in old age, which philosophy ignores. It then asserts that special knowledge is granted to prophets and saints, whereas philosophy posits universal concepts. Philosophy is criticized for evading the nature of memory, while the Quran provides material-free explanations. The origin of discoveries is attributed to a divine light in the Quran, with only Descartes hinting at it. Moral values are treated as absolute axioms in the Quran, but as relative outcomes in philosophy. The inheritance of traits, both good and bad, is recognized by the Quran (citing the examples of the Children of Israel and the family of Yasin), while philosophy ignores it. The existence of devils influencing human decisions is affirmed by the Quran, but philosophy reduces good and evil to relativity. Madness is explained as demonic possession in the Quran, whereas philosophy relegates it to psychology and psychiatry without real explanation. Language is divinely inspired according to the Quran (referencing the teaching of Adam), while philosophy sees it as empirical. Finally, dream visions are considered valuable sources of knowledge in the Quran, but dismissed as meaningless by philosophers. Each point illustrates the author's claim that the Quranic theory is more comprehensive and addresses realities overlooked by philosophy.

Key Terms
نظرية القرآن
نظريات الفلاسفة
المعرفة

C) Rational deduction
D) Universal concepts

Key Takeaway
The Quranic theory posits that knowledge can be divinely gifted, not merely earned, challenging the philosophical assumption of universal rational access.

Unexplained Phenomena: Memory, Madness, and Dreams in Quranic Epistemology
Author shows Quranic explanations for mental phenomena ignored by philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how the Quranic theory provides explanations for memory decline, forgetting, madness, and dream visions, contrasting with philosophical neglect or misattribution.

Core Idea
The Quran gives concrete, non-materialist explanations for mental phenomena that philosophy avoids.

Philosophical theories of knowledge often ignore or reduce phenomena like memory loss, madness, and dreams to purely physical processes—or leave them unexplored. The Quranic theory, however, offers specific explanations: memory decline is recognized, forgetting is explained in non-material terms, madness is attributed to diabolical influence, and dreams are valid sources of knowledge. This lesson examines these four points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic account of memory decline
Find point 20. The Quranic theory notes that memory and mental centers weaken in old age. Philosophy ignores this. Why is this a significant observation for an epistemological theory?
2
Examine the explanation of forgetting
Point 22: Philosophy evades the subject; the Quran gives specific answers and strips it of matter. What does 'stripped of matter' imply?
3
Analyze the Quranic view of madness
Point 26: Madness is domination of devils over the soul. Contrast this with philosophy's referral to psychology/psychiatry. How does this reflect different epistemological assumptions?
4
Evaluate the status of dream visions
Point 28: Quran sees dreams as an important source of knowledge; philosophy dismisses them as subjective. Reflect on how this affects the scope of epistemology.

The Quranic vs Philosophical Foundations of Moral Knowledge
Author's first contrast: moral values

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Quranic view of moral values as fixed axioms from the philosophical view of them as contingent results of knowledge.

Core Idea
The Quran grounds morality in revelation; philosophy grounds it in human reason and circumstance.

The author begins contrasting how the Quran and philosophy treat moral values. For the Quran, moral values are fixed axioms, while philosophy sees them as accidental results. This fundamental difference shapes subsequent epistemological disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about moral values
Find the exact phrase in the passage that states the Quranic view of moral values as fixed axioms and the philosophical view as accidental results.
Point 24: 'The Quranic theory dealt with moral values as fixed axioms and mental postulates, while most philosophical theories dealt with moral values as accidental results of knowledge.'
2
Contrast the two positions
Note the implications: fixed axioms are universal and unchanging; accidental results are relative. How does this affect the status of moral knowledge?
3
Apply to a fiqh principle
Relate this to the usul al-fiqh debate on tahsin wa taqbih: are good and evil known by reason or revelation? The Quranic view aligns with revelation-based ethics.

٢٩ ) قالت نظرية القرآن الكريم ان المعرفة اليقينية ممكنة للبشر ممن طهرت قلوبهم وصفت اذهانهم وقاموا بالرياضات الروحية والمجاهدات النفسية حيث سترتفع الحجب عن هؤلاء فيعرفون اسرار الكون والعالم كمن يرى في المرآة فيرى ما يحتاج او يريد من الماضي او الحاضر او المستقبل في حين وقفت نظريات الفلاسفة عند حدود الاستدلالات والبراهين العقلية. ويمكن استثناء النظرية الصوفية من هذه القاعدة مع اختلافها عن القرآن الكريم في الموارد والمقاصد .
٣٠ ) اختلف القرآن الكريم مع نظرية التحليل النفسي في تقدير قيمة الغريزة الجنسية ففي حين قال القرآن ان لهذه الغريزة اهمية بالغة في بعض قرارات النفس فتزيدها سلبا او ايجابا قالت نظرية التحليل النفسي ان الغريزة الجنسية هي الجوهر والاصل والمسبب للمعرفة .
٣١) الفيلسوف في نظرية المعرفة يريد ان يفهم العالم والعارف يريد ان يصل الى كنه وحقيقة الوجود ويتصل به ويشاهده .
(٣٢) الادوات التي يستخدمها الفيلسوف في حركته العقل والمنطق والاستدلال في حين ان العارف يضيف لها القلب والتصفية والتهذيب.
24) The Quranic theory dealt with moral values as fixed axioms and mental postulates, while most philosophical theories dealt with moral values as accidental results of knowledge. 25) The Quranic theory spoke about the inheritance of negative and positive traits, such as the inheritance of the bad side (as in the case of the Children of Israel) and the inheritance of the positive side (as in the inheritance of the family of Yasin), while philosophical theories of knowledge did not pay attention to this aspect at all. 26) The Quranic theory stated that there are destructive and evil elements that affect the decision made by humans, namely the devils, while philosophical theories considered matters of good and evil as relative issues that change according to temporal and spatial conditions. 26) The Quranic theory stated in knowledge that insanity is the domination of devils over the soul, leading to the collapse of the system of insight, and philosophical theories did not address this topic at all. Philosophers neglected this important epistemological topic and referred it to psychology, which avoided its treatment or interpretation, and turned it back to psychiatry, which sufficed with palliative or anesthetic treatments. 27) Language in philosophical theories is formed through experience, training, and need, while in the Quranic theory its primary basic vocabulary is inspiration from God (And He taught Adam all the names) (He taught him expression). 28) The Holy Quran differed with philosophical theories on the subject of dreams. While the Quran said they are an important source of knowledge, philosophical theories said that dreams are psychological manifestations of no value, and some said that dreams are psychological disturbances. Descartes' acknowledgment of the role of dreams in inspiring him with analytical geometry must be recorded, but this acknowledgment was not intended as a sound methodological scientific indication that dreams are a source of knowledge, but rather as a recording of a strange and wondrous story, which does not give Descartes' acknowledgment any value. Rather, we record this dream as a point of defect in his thinking, not the opposite. 29) The Holy Quranic theory said that certain knowledge is possible for humans whose hearts are purified, their minds clear, and who perform spiritual exercises and psychic struggles, so the veils will be lifted from them and they will know the secrets of the universe and the world like one who sees in a mirror and sees what he needs or wants from the past, present, or future, while philosophical theories stopped at rational inferences and proofs. The Sufi theory can be excepted from this rule, though it differs from the Holy Quran in resources and purposes. 30) The Holy Quran differed with the theory of psychoanalysis in estimating the value of the sexual instinct. While the Quran said that this instinct has great importance in some decisions of the soul, increasing it negatively or positively, the theory of psychoanalysis said that the sexual instinct is the essence, origin, and cause of knowledge. 31) The philosopher in the theory of knowledge wants to understand the world, while the gnostic wants to reach the essence and reality of existence, connect with it, and witness it. 32) The tools used by the philosopher in his movement are intellect, logic, and inference, while the gnostic adds to them the heart, purification, and discipline.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a comparative enumeration by the author of ten points distinguishing the Quranic theory of knowledge from various philosophical theories. The author proceeds by stating each contrast directly. Point 24: The Quran treats moral values as fixed, axiomatic truths, whereas philosophy sees them as contingent byproducts of knowledge. Point 25: The Quran acknowledges hereditary transmission of both positive and negative traits (citing the Israelites and the family of Yasin), which philosophy ignores. Point 26 (first): The Quran affirms the existence of satanic influences on human decision-making, while philosophy relativizes good and evil to time and place. Point 26 (second): The Quran attributes insanity to demonic possession and collapse of insight, while philosophy relegates it to psychology and then to psychiatry, offering only palliative treatments. Point 27: Language, for philosophy, arises from experience, training, and need; for the Quran, it is divinely inspired, as in the verse 'And He taught Adam all the names' and 'He taught him expression.' Point 28: The Quran values dreams as a source of knowledge; philosophy dismisses them as valueless psychic phenomena. The author criticizes Descartes' acknowledgment of a dream inspiring his geometry as a mere curiosity, not a valid epistemological source. Point 29: Certain knowledge is attainable for those who purify heart and mind through spiritual discipline, leading to direct vision of past, present, and future; philosophy limits itself to rational proofs. The author notes an exception for Sufism but emphasizes its divergence from the Quran. Point 30: The Quran acknowledges the sexual instinct's influence on the soul positively or negatively; psychoanalysis treats it as the essence and origin of all knowledge. Point 31: The philosopher seeks to understand the world; the gnostic seeks to reach and witness the reality of existence. Point 32: The philosopher's tools are intellect, logic, and inference; the gnostic adds the heart, purification, and self-discipline.

Key Terms
نظرية المعرفة
القيم الاخلاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the Quranic theory treat moral values?
A) As relative to time and place
B) As fixed axioms and mental postulates
C) As accidental results of knowledge
D) As products of social evolution

Key Takeaway
The Quran’s theory of knowledge treats moral values as fixed, revelation-based axioms, while philosophy treats them as contingent results of knowledge.

?
Read the discussion on tahsin wa taqbih by al-Ghazali in al-Mustasfa and identify whether his position aligns with the Quranic or philosophical view as described in this lesson.

Dreams as a Source of Knowledge in Quranic Epistemology
Author's contrast on dreams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Quranic view of dreams as a legitimate source of knowledge and contrast it with the philosophical dismissal, including an evaluation of Descartes' example.

Core Idea
For the Quran, dreams are a window to truth; for philosophy, they are mere psychological residue.

The author highlights dreams as a point of sharp disagreement. While the Quran considers them an important source, philosophers reduce them to valueless psychic phenomena. Descartes' own dream is cited but dismissed as unsystematic.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Quranic claim about dreams
Identify the positive statement: the Quran says dreams are an important source of knowledge.
From point 28: 'the Quran said they are an important source of knowledge'
2
Identify the philosophical counter-claim
Find the philosophical view: dreams are psychological manifestations of no value or disturbances.
Same point: 'philosophical theories said that dreams are psychological manifestations of no value'
3
Evaluate the author's critique of Descartes
Why does the author dismiss Descartes' acknowledgment? Because it was not a systematic epistemological claim but an anecdote.
The passage says Descartes' acknowledgment was 'not intended as a sound methodological scientific indication' but 'a strange and wondrous story'.
